Faris also is a 1997 Cedarville University graduate, according to the school&rsquo;s website. Faris did not name the individual who she said harassed her and she did not name the station. The View airs on ABC 22 in Dayton. The station&rsquo;s general manager could not be reached to comment. &ldquo;I kind of thought what doesn&rsquo;t kill you makes you stronger but on my way out, about a year later I got another job,&rdquo; Faris said. &ldquo;&hellip;So I just wrote a letter to the human resources department on my way out saying that this guy was abusing people verbally and I wasn&rsquo;t in it for litigation or money or anything, I don&rsquo;t know what happened.&rdquo; Faris&rsquo; comments were made as hosts of The View were discussing the sexual harassment scandal that led to Bill O&rsquo;Reilly being fired from Fox News earlier this week. The View showed a clip of former Republican vice-presidential candidate Sarah Palin saying that women who are sexually harassed should speak out. &ldquo;Sometimes it&rsquo;s really hard&rdquo; for victims of sexual harassment to speak up, Faris said, before telling the story of her own experience. Faris told the Dayton Daily News she did not want to share further details of the harassment incident. &ldquo;This was roughly 16 years ago. And, is really water under the bridge, as far as I&rsquo;m concerned,&rdquo; Faris said via email. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not interested in publicly naming someone and assassinating their character, further. I shared with the sole intent of hoping others, women and men, would be more courageous in sharing their own stories.&rdquo; Brian Butler, an attorney at Mezibov-Butler law firm in Cincinnati, said that under federal law, cases of sexual harassment have to be reported within 300 days for legal action to be taken. Ohio law allows for a case of sexual harassment to be reported within 6 years, Butler said. Since Faris was harassed nearly 16 years ago, legally, &ldquo;really nothing can be done,&rdquo; Butler said. 5 QUICK READS &bull; Ohio State wants to offer more jobs to Ohioans with disabilities &bull; market tests out expanded hours. Three out of four market vendors have committed to opening on Sundays during a 13-week pilot program that starts at the beginning of June, said Jimmy Harless, manager of the 2nd Street Market. Most of the market&rsquo;s major vendors will be open for business, and every type of seller will be represented on Sundays, including three outside farmer vendors, Harless said. Pavilion vendors, who sell crafts, art, jewelry and other creations, also will have a strong representation, and musicians will be present, playing for tips, Harless said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re going to offer pretty much everything we offer on Saturday,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll have more of a Saturday feel with the outside market, with music for entertainment.&rdquo; RELATED: 2nd Street Market to test expanded hours Some vendors who plan on being open on Sundays include Caffeine Carl, the Flowerman, KJB Farm, the Olive Tree, the Chef Case, Crepe Boh&egrave;me, Spice Rack & Bulk Foods, Cheeky Meat Pies, Tim&rsquo;s Gifts N More, New World Alpaca Textiles, Dayton Urban Green, Animal Snackers Bakery, Arepas & Co. Express and Azra&rsquo;s Mediterranean Cuisine. &ldquo;We are so excited and so ready for this,&rdquo; said Azra Kaurin, owner of Azra&rsquo;s Mediterranean Cuisine. Five Rivers MetroParks&rsquo; pilot program, if successful, could lead the market to not only bring back Sunday hours in 2018 but also possibly expand its hours of operation further, officials said. Success will be measured by attendance, feedback and vendor participation. For this year, pulling Friday attendance figures on a Sunday would be an achievement, said Amy Forsthoefel, Five Rivers MetroParks&rsquo; research and analysis manager. That equates to about 1,000 to 1,200 people every Sunday. It&rsquo;s unclear if the market will be able to reach that benchmark, but officials expect Sundays to feel a lot more like Saturdays than either Thursdays or Fridays. Saturdays draw about 5,000 visitors during the peak parts of summer. RELATED: Will Sunday hours at 2nd Street Market work? For years, the buzz around the market was that vendors would love the opportunity to open on Sundays. Now that they have that chance, vendors have lined up to participate in the pilot program, which runs June 4 to Aug. 27. Customers also have expressed lots of interest in Sunday hours.    \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  The one-year anniversary of the brutal slayings of eight family members in rural Pike County is April 22.&nbsp; Despite intense media scrutiny and around-the-clock work by investigators, there still remain more questions than answers.&nbsp; Here are five mysteries that still remain one year later: &#65279;At various times in the investigation there have been differing theories on who killed the Rhoden family.&nbsp; After a previous drug bust in the area was mentioned, statements by Ohio Attorney General Mike DeWine led to speculation that a Mexican drug cartel could have been involved. Commercial marijuana grow operations were found at two of the four murder scenes.&nbsp; But DeWine and Pike County Sheriff Charles Reader have since expressed the belief that the perpetrators &ndash; the consensus is that there&rsquo;s more than one &ndash; were local to Pike County. Some officials have speculated the suspects were even closer to the family. Leonard Manley, whose daughter Dana Rhoden, 37, was one of the eight victims, has previously said whoever entered the property had to be familiar to the family&rsquo;s dogs, or else they would have barked and attacked.&nbsp; The victims suffered a combined 32 gunshot wounds &ndash; one was shot nine times, two were shot five times each &ndash; and some showed soft tissue bruising, suggesting they may have been beaten, according to autopsy reports. DeWine and Reader would not say at a recent press conference if they believe they&rsquo;ve interviewed the perpetrator(s).&nbsp; DeWine and Reader gave a press conference April 13 and stressed that the case is still a top priority.&nbsp; But after more than 800 tips, 400 interviews, 38 search warrants and a dozen investigators constantly on the ground in Piketon, there have been no arrests.&nbsp; DeWine and Reader declined to discuss possible motives, a time-frame for the investigation or scenarios that have been ruled out.&nbsp; They insist a lack of disclosure about new details will protect the integrity of the investigation &mdash; the largest in the Ohio Bureau of Criminal Investigation history. They also insist the case is not cold, and that there exists &mdash; somewhere in the county, or elsewhere &mdash; someone with answers. &ldquo;We will find you,&rdquo; Reader said to the killer or killers. &ldquo;We will arrest you, and you will be prosecuted.&rdquo; The sheriff&rsquo;s office has hung posters all in various locations around Piketon, advertising a $10,000 reward for information that leads to an arrest in the case.&nbsp; Crime Stoppers is accepting donations to increase that amount.&nbsp; But Reader admitted at the recent press conference that fear of reprisal is still a real factor in this case.&nbsp; He said he&rsquo;s sure some of the people they&rsquo;ve interviewed have lied to them, whether out of fear of getting in trouble for criminal activity, or out of fear that they could be next.&nbsp; The killings were committed&nbsp;&ldquo;execution-style,&rdquo; and whoever did it is still out there. In the immediate aftermath of the crimes, Reader told other members of the Rhoden family to arm themselves for protection.&nbsp; DeWine said solving the murders is the top priority, and detectives need information. &ldquo;This is a homicide investigation,&rdquo; he said.&nbsp;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s not that we don&rsquo;t care what you&rsquo;ve done with drugs &ndash; we care, but our focus is on the homicide, and so people should not be concerned about coming forward and disclosing information that may be helpful in the investigation.&rdquo; Was the family killed by rival drug dealers or were the murders in connection to other criminal activity? There was evidence they were raising roosters for cock fighting, DeWine has said. This criminal activity, Reader said, was &ldquo;minute&rdquo; compared to their slaughter. &ldquo;Regardless of their lifestyle, they were human beings,&rdquo; he said.&nbsp; Was the murder more personal? In the immediate aftermath, several individuals who had expressed prior disputes&nbsp;with Rhoden family members on social media were questioned, but quickly let go.&nbsp; There have been more rumors than concrete theories when it comes to motive.&nbsp; Things are quieter now in the rural community, but the specter of these slayings, unsolved, still hangs over the people of Pike County. A billboard with the victim&rsquo;s faces literally hovers over the sheriff&rsquo;s office.&nbsp; &ldquo;I see the look of disappointment when I speak with the family, and I look into their eyes and the grieving they still have,&rdquo; Reader said last week. He said these murders were the worst thing he&rsquo;s seen in all his years in law enforcement.&nbsp; &ldquo;There will always be a scar on this town,&rdquo; said&nbsp;Morty Throckmorton, who manages the Smart Mart store in Piketon. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/pike-county-murders-unanswered-questions-year-later/f5PnS126ILLe1O43mvjrCN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/19/Videos/4032325.vpx\" title=\"Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later \" alt=\"Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/pike-county-murders-unanswered-questions-year-later/f5PnS126ILLe1O43mvjrCN/\" target=\"\">Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nKatie Wedell</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The one-year anniversary of the brutal slayings of eight family members in rural Pike County is April 22. Would you believe Jack Nicklaus once played in a significant golf tournament at Dayton&rsquo;s Madden Golf Course. If not, you can start believing. Nicklaus played in the Ohio Open Championship at Madden in 1958 when he was 18. He had won the Ohio Open in 1956 at the age of 16, but the Dayton event was won by Bob Shave. privacy protections for Internet users, the principals behind Dayton-based &lsquo;Net services firm DataYard had a simple message: DataYard will not sell customer information. Period. As much as they opposed the legislation, DataYard principals (and father and son) David and Alek Mezera, say they saw the government&rsquo;s move as a chance to distinguish their company. &ldquo;They (other companies) can collect it, they can store it for indefinite periods of time and they can do what they see fit with it, when they decide to do so,&rdquo; said Alek Mezera, the younger Mezera and the company&rsquo;s director of client partnerships. &ldquo;We will not collect it,&rdquo; said David Mezera, company president. &ldquo;We will not keep it for any longer than is necessary to deliver the services we deliver. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re absolutely not interested in monetizing that information.&rdquo; The federal privacy rules would have taken effect this year. Enshrined by the previous administration, they would have banned Internet providers from collecting, sharing and mostly importantly, selling user information without consent. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think was a surprise, really,&rdquo; the younger Mezera said of Congress&rsquo; push back against the rules. Ajit Pai &mdash; nominated by the Trump administration this year to be FCC chairman &mdash; has said the Federal Trade Commission, not the FCC, should regulate how Internet providers use data. Now in its 22nd year, DataYard was born as Donet, with all of eight phone lines in the beginning. It re-branded as DataYard in 2012. &ldquo;We were taking a more strategic business focus, instead of the residential consumer market that we initially started with in &lsquo;95&rdquo; DataYard&rsquo;s president said. The business since then has transformed. Today with 13 employees, the focus goes beyond Internet access. Offered also are server co-location, cloud computing, data backup and more. The Internet back in the mid-90s was basically a hobby, the Mezeras recalled. Today, it is threaded into our lives in ways few imagined back then. &ldquo;Now, the Internet is an essential utility,&rdquo; the older Mezera said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s an essential element of every business.&rdquo; Based downtown at 130 W. Second St., the company is bigger today but remains local &mdash; and unmistakeably Daytonian. The focus is not necessarily on ever-growing sales, but on building relationships with customers. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re not trying to get a national footprint or a global footprint,&rdquo; Dave said. Why not aim to get bigger? &ldquo;It&rsquo;s harder to maintain those personal relationships,&rdquo; the elder Mezera said. &ldquo;You lose that flavor.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a race to the bottom \n<!-- map content:  Around the Miami Valley, local governments and community activists are rallying against plans by the Trump administration to eliminate a 43-year-old federal program that has brought multi-millions of dollars of assistance to lower-income neighborhoods in the area. Under President Donald Trump&rsquo;s proposed budget, the Community Development Block Grant (CDBG) Program could be eliminated as part of $6 billion in cuts in the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD). &ldquo;We are concerned it may go away,&rdquo; said Mary Crockett, community development coordinator in the City of Xenia. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t see anything replacing it.&rdquo; Last week in Lebanon, the Warren County Board of Commissioners delayed action on $700,000 in CDBG grant requests until a decision is made on the future of the program. RELATED: \n            \n            \n               Trump budget takes aim at dozens of government programs Dayton has received more than $57 million in CDBG funds since 2007, used to knock down blighted properties, resurface residential streets, enforce city code and help secure and upgrade housing for the homeless, the elderly, veterans and low-income families. RELATED: Dayton, Montgomery County leaders oppose Trump budget cut plan &ldquo;It&rsquo;s one of the most effective uses of federal dollars,&rdquo; Dayton Mayor Nan Whaley said earlier this month. Montgomery County, which received $8.5 million from the CDBG program in the last five years, uses the money for infrastructure projects, demolition, housing rehab and small business grants and upgrades for places to become compliant with the American with Disabilities Act. &ldquo;If that funding goes away, it&rsquo;s going to be devastating, especially in the urban areas,&rdquo; said Michael Colbert, assistant Montgomery County administrator. Trump proposes pulling all funding for the CDBG program, which has provided more than $150 billion to U.S. communities since its inception in 1974. &ldquo;We are going to do more with less, and make the government lean and accountable to the people,&rdquo; Trump said in the budget document. Last month, U.S. Rep. Mike Turner, R-Dayton, signed a &ldquo;dear colleague&rdquo; letter urging a Congressional subcommittee on appropriations to provide at least $3.3 billion in funding for the CDBG program in fiscal year 2018, according to the National Low Income Housing Coalition. On Friday, Turner&rsquo;s office said nothing had changed. Other co-sponsors included U.S. Reps. Joyce Beatty, D-Jefferson Twp., Marcia Fudge, D-Warrensville Heights; and Lou Barletta, R-Pa. Dayton is among Ohio cities that apply to HUD in Washington D.C. Other local governments, including counties and municipalities, apply through the Ohio Department of Development Services. RELATED: Dayton&rsquo;s 5-year plan for CDBG funds Greene County is going ahead with plans to apply for CDBG funds through the state, despite concerns about the program&rsquo;s future. On May 4, Kristie Tidd, the county&rsquo;s community development coordinator, plans to outline for commissioners a list of $200,000 in requests, ranging from $48,500 for Jamestown Opera House renovations to $25,000 for a revolving loan fund for low-income home repairs in Yellow Springs. &ldquo;We are proud supporters of this program. It&rsquo;s very helpful to our communities,&rdquo; Tidd said, adding she was contacting area recipients, encouraging them to contact their lawmakers about the proposed program elimination. While uncertain about the funding&rsquo;s future, Xenia and Warren County officials already moved forward with public hearings required to qualify for assistance through the federal program, signed into law by President Richard M. Nixon. In past years, Xenia has received $75,000 to $80,000 a year. Before the cuts proposed by Trump, the city was anticipating $150,000 every two years under a new process begun this year. Xenia officials are weighing whether to pursue another HUD grant, while hoping the CDBG program is funded in 2018, when it again expected to benefit from the program, Crockett said. &ldquo;We may just continue planning for next year,&rdquo; Crockett said, noting CDBG funds provided for loans used to redo the facades of 10 downtown buildings. Warren County commissioners signed off on a list of recipients seeking about $700,000, based on recent annual allocations for projects in low-income areas of the generally affluent county. RELATED: Warren County holds off on $700,000 in grant requests Also, Matt Schnipke, the county&rsquo;s economic development specialist, was cleared to promote continuation of the program during the upcoming Warren County Chamber Alliance&rsquo;s annual fly-in to Washington D.C. MORE: Warren County Chamber Alliance&rsquo;s first Washington D.C. lobbying trip The reaction in Warren County, a Republican stronghold, shows bipartisan support for the program. This year&rsquo;s list is topped by $277,900 for repairs on Cambridge Court, Georgetown Drive, Raintree Trail and Winding Way, streets within lower-income neighborhoods in Lebanon. The program also would provide $160,000 to fix Harriet and Olive avenues and Mentz Road in Franklin and $90,000 for local administration. It also included funds for a playground upgrade in Butlerville, among other projects. RELATED: No federal grant for Franklin museum SOCIAL MEDIA:Follow Lawrence Budd on Twitter News of CDBG cuts emerged last month when the Trump administration proposed general budgets for federal agencies, including HUD. DOWNLOAD OUR FREE MOBILE APPS FOR THE LATEST BREAKING NEWS &ldquo;The blueprint reflects the President&rsquo;s commitment to support HUD&rsquo;s critical functions that provide rental assistance to low-income and vulnerable households and to help work-eligible families achieve self-sufficiency. It also recognizes a greater role for state and local governments, and the private sector to address community and economic development needs. &rdquo; according to a statement on the HUD web site. &rdquo;A more detailed program-by-program budget proposal will be announced in May.&rdquo; \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Three candidates for Huber Heights mayor will face off in Ohio&rsquo;s May 2 primary election. Jeff Gore, Albert Griggs Jr. and David Wilson are candidates to fill the seat currently held by Mayor Tom McMasters, who is not seeking re-election. Voters in the primary will cast ballots for one candidate, and the top two will compete head-to-head in November&rsquo;s general election. MORE: Huber Heights to pick new mayor The candidates collectively identified economic development and growth among their top priorities. The Dayton Daily News asked the candidates to fill out a voter guide of questions crafted by the newsroom. Portions of those responses are part of this story, and the full voter guide is available online at vote.daytondailynews.com. There, the candidates weighed in on issues including the decision to forego the Montgomery County Fairgrounds, the role of social media among elected officials, and residential chickens. Top priorities The newspaper asked the candidates to name their top three priorities if elected. Responses generally touched on economic development and growth. Gore said his top priorities are making sure residents have a good understanding of the city&rsquo;s financial situation, restoring relationships with the business community to promote economic growth, and strengthening and enforcing the city&rsquo;s zoning codes and ordinances. MORE: Huber Heights mayor won&rsquo;t seek re-election Griggs said his top priorities are developing effective and efficient city government, strong public schools and continued economic growth for the city. Wilson&rsquo;s top three priorities are revitalizing parks, economic development and responsible growth. Council disagreements In past months, council members have engaged in disagreements, insults and accusations between members. Council members often refer to themselves as &ldquo;old council&rdquo; or &ldquo;new council&rdquo; to describe their perspectives. The newspaper asked candidates to identify the major source of the disagreement and how they would lessen fractures. Gore said he believes fresh ideas and perspectives are good, but experience and knowledge are also needed. &ldquo;Based on my observations, the source of the disagreement comes from differing visions for the city,&rdquo; Gore said. &ldquo;I think it&rsquo;s great that we have competing visions, and I think there is room for disagreement.&rdquo; He suggested leadership is needed to promote compromise. MORE: Incumbents seek new seats on Huber council Griggs also said council needs to work to find common ground, but he also said he sees the disagreements as each council member&rsquo;s passion for what he or she believes. &ldquo;People with passion are what we need on the council,&rdquo; Griggs said. &ldquo;The question is, &lsquo;what is the best way to show this passion?&rsquo; I believe you work to understand the passion.&rdquo; Wilson focused on the non-partisanship of the council and said his job as mayor would &ldquo;not include divining anyone as working for one &lsquo;team&rsquo; or another.&rdquo; &ldquo;My job as mayor is to treat all members of council equally and with respect,&rdquo; he said. MORE: Dayton Daily News voter guide Future growth The candidates generally agreed Huber Heights should seek measured growth. Gore emphasized taking advantage of the city&rsquo;s proximity to the Interstate highways &ldquo;or our neighbors will.&rdquo; Griggs said there is &ldquo;no such thing as too much growth,&rdquo; but said it should be &ldquo;controlled to ensure infrastructure keeps up with it.&rdquo; Wilson said he believes the city will primarily see increases in residential housing and light industry in the next 10 years. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local-govt--politics/seek-next-huber-heights-mayor/FSR8M99tXH6IkvrDrPl8sM/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18461985_vote-art.jpg\" title=\"3 seek to be next Huber Heights mayor \" alt=\"3 seek to be next Huber Heights mayor \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local-govt--politics/seek-next-huber-heights-mayor/FSR8M99tXH6IkvrDrPl8sM/\" target=\"\">3 seek to be next Huber Heights mayor </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nWill Garbe, <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Three candidates for Huber Heights mayor will face off in Ohio’s May 2 primary election. Jeff Gore, Albert Griggs Jr. and David Wilson are candidates to fill the seat currently held by Mayor Tom McMasters, who is not seeking re-election. Voters in the primary will cast ballots for one candidate, and the top two will compete head-to-head in November’s general election.       \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  CHESTERFIELD COUNTY, Va. &mdash;&nbsp;The police brought cadaver dogs to search the small redbrick rancher in this suburban community south of Richmond. On that March day more than a year ago, they were seeking &quot;any evidence associated with the delivery and death of a newborn child whether alive or deceased,&quot; according to the affidavit for the search warrant.&nbsp; What they found buried in the back yard - the skeletal remains of a fetus - led to the indictment last month of a 43-year-old woman who lived there. Michelle Frances Roberts is accused of self-aborting in the third trimester of her pregnancy. She was arrested March 29 this year on felony charges of &quot;producing abortion or miscarriage.&quot;&nbsp; The rarely invoked law, written in 1950, prohibits giving a pregnant woman a drug or taking any action &quot;with intent to destroy her unborn child.&quot;&nbsp; Roberts, who remains in jail in Chesterfield, has denied the charges. According to the police, she told investigators she gave birth to a baby boy in the house, but he died afterward, and she buried him in the back yard. Police and prosecutors have revealed little about the evidence that led to the unusual charge against Roberts, who court records show has a history of drug use.&nbsp; Commonwealth Assistant Attorney Shawn Gobble refused to comment on the case. Roberts' defense attorney, H. Pratt Cook III, did not respond to repeated requests for comment. She is scheduled to appear in court Monday for a status hearing on the case, which has alarmed women's rights advocates in Virginia. &quot;It is unclear from the questionable, selective release of information by the Chesterfield police exactly what happened here,&quot; said Gail Deady, the Secular Society Women's Rights legal fellow for the Virginia ACLU. &quot;But it appears that this is another example of overreach and targeting of a pregnant woman in an attempt to shame and punish her for her circumstances.&quot; But a neighbor who saw the police cars arrive on their street said the discovery of the remains near a big tree in the back yard sent chills through her body: &quot;It wasn't right. She should have gone to a doctor. If you don't want the baby, you go to the doctor.&quot; The law has been used at least once before by Virginia authorities to charge a woman with harming her unborn child, according to the Richmond Times-Dispatch. In 2005, a Suffolk woman was prosecuted for shooting herself in the abdomen on her due date and then blaming her injury on a man. But a circuit court judge dismissed the charge, saying the law was not intended to punish expectant mothers. It was aimed at the actions of others.&nbsp; Repeated efforts by state legislators to pass a law making it a crime for a pregnant woman to self-abort were unsuccessful.&nbsp; Deady said she is concerned about the repercussions of the Roberts prosecution.&nbsp; &quot;It could mean anyone who has a miscarriage could be subjected to an investigation by police and thrown in jail,&quot; she argued. It was the mother of Michelle Roberts's boyfriend, Danny Coppola, who tipped police off to the missing baby, according to court records.&nbsp; Before Coppola went to prison in 2015 for violating his probation in a 2011 forgery case, he told his mother, Linda Castillo, that Roberts was pregnant with a baby boy, according to police. The couple also have an adult daughter, Lauren Coppola.&nbsp; Roberts, police said, acknowledged to Castillo that she was five months pregnant. Castillo asked Roberts which doctor she was going to. &quot;Michelle told her that she was going to check with the doctor at the methadone clinic because they would not judge her,&quot; police said.&nbsp; Since 2012, Roberts had been visiting the Richmond Southside Treatment Center, where she received low doses of methadone four times a week, police said. Detectives said the clinic director verified Roberts was a regular patient at the clinic, but he had no record of Roberts ever being pregnant. By Castillo's calculations, her new grandchild should have been born around September or October 2015. But she never received an announcement. Castillo told police she next saw Roberts in October 2015 at a hospital where Lauren Coppola had been admitted after a car accident. At the hospital, Castillo asked Lauren about her mother's pregnancy, police said. Coppola, now 21, told her grandmother that she &quot;needed to talk to Michelle about that,&quot; police said, &quot;and that she didn't want to be involved.&quot; Castillo walked into the hospital hallway, where she saw Roberts. She noticed Roberts no longer appeared to be pregnant, police said.&nbsp; Roberts told Castillo, &quot;This isn't anything we need to discuss here.&quot; About a month later, Coppola and her boyfriend, Dylan Kidwell, had an argument at the house on Philbrook Road in Chesterfield, where they lived with Roberts and Roberts's stepfather, police said. Castillo arrived during the tumult. At one point, Kidwell shouted something at his girlfriend's mother that made Castillo suspicious, police said.&nbsp; &quot;You better watch yourself,&quot; Kidwell yelled at Roberts. &quot;Because I know what's buried in the back yard.&quot; Castillo asked Roberts what was buried in the back yard.&nbsp; &quot;Michelle told Linda that the baby had been born in the house,&quot; according to police.&quot;She said the baby took one breath, at which point she passed out,&quot; police said. &quot;Michelle said that Lauren woke her up and told her that the baby died and that she and Dylan dug a hole in the back yard and buried it.&quot; Castillo died on March 12 last year, five days after police executed a warrant to search the back yard of the house on Philbrook. Castillo was 56. Chesterfield County police said Lauren Coppola and Dylan Kidwell have not been charged in the case. Neither could be reached for comment. &quot;At this time Michelle Roberts is the only one charged in this matter,&quot; said Capt. G. Netherland, a spokesman with the Chesterfield County police. Before his mother's death, Danny Coppola, who is incarcerated at the Augusta Correctional Center in Craigsville, Virginia, wrote three letters to her in which he mentioned &quot;Michelle's baby,&quot; police said. The police search of the home and the back yard, court records say, yielded: &quot;human remains; two Samsung galaxy cellphones; a disc thumb drive; a white tablet; and a journal.&quot; The house, where pink azalea bushes are blooming in the front yard, is now for sale. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/virginia-woman-faces-rare-charge-self-induced-abortion/tiM5UlCQ5owZqHEsUEaU4N/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Wires/w2/WashingtonPost/2017/04/21/Images/virginia-arrest-2e21f54a-2689-11e7-a1b3-faff0034e2de.jpg\" title=\"Virginia woman faces rare charge of self-induced abortion \" alt=\"Virginia woman faces rare charge of self-induced abortion \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/virginia-woman-faces-rare-charge-self-induced-abortion/tiM5UlCQ5owZqHEsUEaU4N/\" target=\"\">Virginia woman faces rare charge of self-induced abortion </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nDeNeen L. \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Silicon Valley firms have long been known for offering a litany of employee perks: Home-cooked lunches, free massages, climbing walls and dog-friendly offices. Now some are adding yet another incentive to attract -- and retain -- workers: Paid time off to protest. Fauna, a San Francisco-based database start-up, recently began allowing its 13 employees to take unlimited paid leave to participate in rallies, vote, write letters to elected officials and take part in other civic activities. Before February, employees could take time off on an as-needed basis. But the political climate -- and polarization -- following President Donald Trump's inauguration called for more defined measures, said Amna Pervez, director of recruiting and retention. &quot;Since there's been such a divide in our country, we felt we should be very explicit about our policy,&quot; Pervez said, adding that the company also provides unlimited vacation time. &quot;We want our employees to know that we absolutely support the betterment of our country. People can take whatever they feel like they need to make a meaningful difference.&quot; A number of other start-ups, including Turbine Labs, Buoyant and Jelly Industries, have signed on to do the same. The new policies come as technology firms and other companies take a stand against the Trump administration's plan to tighten restrictions for foreign workers. On Tuesday, Trump was expected to sign an executive order that would impose new restrictions on H1-B visas, a type of temporary work visa often used by firms to recruit and employ highly-skilled workers. &quot;If you're a tech company, taking a pro-immigration stand is not exactly a bold move,&quot; said Peter Cappelli, a management professor at the Wharton School. &quot;When there's a tight labor market and companies are fighting for programmers, this is a way to say, 'See? We're here to support you.' &quot; Facebook, for example, is allowing its employees to take time off to participate in pro-immigration rallies on May 1. The company, which relies heavily on foreign workers, informed employees and contractors last week that they would not be penalized for missing work to protest, Bloomberg reported Tuesday. &quot;At Facebook, we're committed to fostering an inclusive workplace where employees feel comfortable expressing their opinions and speaking up about issues that are important to them,&quot; a company spokesman said in an email. &quot;We support our people in recognizing International Workers' Day and other efforts to raise awareness for safe and equitable employment conditions.&quot; Facebook could be particularly hard-hit by any changes the Trump administration makes to H-1B visa policies. More than 15 percent of its employees used temporary work visas last year -- a higher percentage than at Google, Apple, Amazon or Microsoft, according to a Reuters analysis of U.S. Labor Department filings. At Atipica, a software start-up in San Francisco, four of the company's five U.S.-based workers are immigrants. Founder Laura G&oacute;mez, who is from Mexico, said it was a &quot;no-brainer&quot; to give workers paid leave to make their voices heard. &quot;At this point in our political reality, it's really, really important to allow my employees to do something that not only affects them, but also the direction of our country,&quot; she said. &quot;This is what democracy looks like: People having the freedom to stand up for what they believe in.&quot; There are some ground rules, though: No violence, or activities that make others feel threatened. &quot;We will define this as we grow,&quot; G&oacute;mez said. &quot;But my hope is that policies like this become the norm. When Google began giving out free lunches, everyone else followed. Why should this be any different?&quot; For her part, G&oacute;mez said, she plans to participate in a pro-immigration march on May 1. &quot;I'll be out there,&quot; she said. &quot;And hopefully the rest of the company will, too.&quot; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/business/newest-silicon-valley-perk-paid-time-off-protest-trump/hY2De1kCfijphRYp99zNhP/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/CmgSharedContent/2017/04/15/Images/GettyImages-668894156%20(1)-R1gPIhahWQHmS5Tz3iKPsgK-680x383%40AJC.com.jpg\" title=\"Newest Silicon Valley perk: paid time off to protest Trump\" alt=\"Newest Silicon Valley perk: paid time off to protest Trump\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/business/newest-silicon-valley-perk-paid-time-off-protest-trump/hY2De1kCfijphRYp99zNhP/\" target=\"\">Newest Silicon Valley perk: paid time off to protest Trump</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nAbha Bhattarai</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Silicon Valley firms have long been known for offering a litany of employee perks: Home-cooked lunches, free massages, climbing walls and dog-friendly offices. Now some are adding yet another incentive to attract -- and retain -- workers: Paid time off to protest. Fauna, a San Francisco-based database start-up, recently began allowing its 13 employees to take unlimited paid leave to participate in...</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t 140&rdquo; Afghan soldiers in the wake of Friday&rsquo;s attack on a military base by Taliban members apparently disguised in military uniforms, officials said. &gt;&gt; Read more trending news One official in the northern city of Mazar-i-Sharif, where the attack occurred, said Saturday at least 140 soldiers were killed and many others were wounded,&nbsp;Reuters reported. Other officials said the toll could even be higher. A U.S. official in Washington on Friday had put the toll at more than 50 killed and wounded, Reuters reported. As many as 10 Taliban fighters, dressed in Afghan army uniforms and driving military vehicles, slipped onto the base and opened fire on mostly unarmed soldiers eating a meal and leaving a mosque after Friday prayers, officials told Reuters. The base is the headquarters for the Afghan National Army's 209th Corps, responsible for much of northern Afghanistan, including Kunduz province where there has been heavy fighting. Taliban spokesman Zabihullah Mujahid said in a statement on Saturday the attack was retribution for the recent killing of several senior Taliban leaders in northern Afghanistan. &quot;The attack on the 209th Corps today shows the barbaric nature of the Taliban,&quot; U.S. Gen. John Nicholson, the commander of coalition forces, said in a statement on Friday. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a \n    \n<!-- map content:  An 11-year NFL career that included five Super Bowl appearances and three Pro Bowl selections provided Matt Light some of his greatest moments in life. But reminiscing about effort, determination and ultimately success on a different field had Matt Light beaming Friday evening. Light, the Greenville High School graduate and former offensive lineman with the New England Patriots, was kicking off his ninth annual Youth Wild Turkey Hunt at the Fraternal Order of Eagles near Greenville. Light, himself a lifelong hunter, told the story of dragging a young hunter named Maria Coyle &ldquo;through the mud and the muck&rdquo; on a difficult outing a few years ago. &ldquo;Her boots came off three times. She never stopped. She never quit. She got frustrated. I think we both did. But when she ended up getting that bird it was one of the coolest moments I&rsquo;ve ever seen,&rdquo; Light said. Light&rsquo;s Youth Wild Turkey Hunt selects 16 Darke County residents between the ages of 12-17 to spend a weekend with Light and some of turkey hunting&rsquo;s biggest stars. They gathered at the FOE for the official send-off Friday night. The program is one of a handful sponsored by the Light Foundation. Participants are judged and selected on essays. During the weekend the hunters learn how to use turkey calls and decoys, gun safety, youth leadership and when to take a safe shot. &ldquo;Every year I&rsquo;m reminded of why we do it,&rdquo; Light said. &ldquo;I forget most of the hunts I do it when it&rsquo;s just me. But I can honestly do a play-by-play on every hunt I&rsquo;ve had with these kids.&rdquo; Arcanum seventh-grader Allison Whiting participated in the hunt last year. She didn&rsquo;t bag a turkey but remains confident she&rsquo;ll get one this time with the single-shot 20-gauge shotgun. &ldquo;Yeah, I&rsquo;m getting one this year,&rdquo; Whiting said. &ldquo;It was really fun last year. I made a lot of new friends.&rdquo; Light calls on a few of his own to help make the event a success. Among the guides are Jamestown&rsquo;s Bob Fulcher, a national champion turkey caller, and Ernie Calandrelli of Quaker Boy Game Calls. Through the year Light&rsquo;s 450-acre Chenoweth Trails facility will serve more than 5,000 people. He said he has three local employees on the payroll. The rest are volunteers, like the 40 who will help this weekend as guides, spotters and drivers to get the hunters into the field. About 30 land owners also allow the hunters to use their property. &ldquo;If these kids knew how many hours everyone spent on their behalf they&rsquo;d be blown away,&rdquo; Light said. &ldquo;It tells you the job of the team that I&rsquo;ve got. While I may not have a quarterback (New England&rsquo;s Tom Brady) wearing No. 12, I&rsquo;ve got arguably a more impressive roster with the guys we do have. The work they&rsquo;re doing is really life changing. Some of these kids who have been on the turkey hunt have asked to come back and help out. To me that speaks volumes.&rdquo; About 45 Darke County youth submitted applications this year, Light said. The process opens in December and applications are available at mattlight72.com. Hunters fortunate enough to land a turkey are required to tell the group their story. Embellishment isn&rsquo;t frowned upon. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a brotherhood and a sisterhood,&rdquo; Light said. &ldquo;The camaraderie of being around each other and enjoying camp and telling hunt stories, which is big for us. Every kid that shoots a bird has to get up and tell the story. It gets a little animated and just like every fish gets bigger, every turkey hunt story gets better. &hellip; Turkeys gobble, they look goofy and they taste great. You can&rsquo;t go wrong with that combination.&rdquo; About 10 turkeys were taken last year. Hunters have the option of having them mounted or breasted. &ldquo;I think it&rsquo;ll be one of those things they look back on and say, &lsquo;Man, that was a cool moment in my life.&rsquo; And maybe it becomes a lifelong addiction,&rdquo; Light said. &ldquo;You have one opportunity to seal the deal. \n<!-- map content:  The city of Springfield won&rsquo;t be resuming its red light camera program any time soon, despite Dayton&rsquo;s proposal to turn its red-light and speed-detection cameras back on later this month to improve safety at intersections. Springfield leaders have said they won&rsquo;t turn the city&rsquo;s cameras back on until the issue is settled statewide in order to avoid driver confusion. The city has no intention of changing its policy, Springfield City Manager Jim Bodenmiller said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;d have to go back to the city commission before that happens,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I certainly support that direction, but we&rsquo;ve not chosen to do that here at this time.&rdquo; Springfield has 17 cameras at 10 intersections that were turned off in 2015 once a new state law went into effect requiring a police officer to be present at the time of the violation. Those regulations have led to multiple lawsuits, including one filed by the city of Springfield, that allege the new rules violate local home-rule authority. The city had issued about 77,000 citations between 2006 and 2015, collecting about $3.4 million in fines. Under the new law, Springfield estimated it would have to hire at least 42 officers to run its 17 cameras at 10 intersections. In Dayton, traffic fatalities have increased 45 percent and crashes have increased 40 percent since 2014, city leaders said. The Dayton Police Department has proposed having 10 fixed cameras, six hand-held devices and two portable trailer units where officers would be present to improve safety at high-crash intersections. Springfield hasn&rsquo;t removed its cameras, which Bodenmiller believes remains a deterrent to some drivers. &ldquo;The safer the streets are, the better,&rdquo; he said. In 2007, the intersections with red light cameras saw 90 accidents. That number decreased to 44 in 2014 &mdash; a 51 percent decrease. A year later, the cameras were turned off after rule changes. Overall traffic crashes increased 8 percent between 2014 and 2016 and traffic crashes at monitored intersections increased 13 percent from 44 in 2014 to 50 in 2016, according to the Springfield Police Division. &ldquo;The facts speak for themselves,&rdquo; Bodenmiller said. &ldquo;I still believe in the concept.&rdquo; The city has already seen three crashes with four fatalities this year, Sgt. Brett Bauer said, two more than all of last year. It&rsquo;s on pace to have more than the 10 that occurred in 2015, which Bauer said was the most he&rsquo;s seen during his time on the force. The Ohio Supreme Court likely will consider Springfield&rsquo;s challenge to the new red light camera laws later this year. Springfield is one of several cities that sued. Springfield&rsquo;s arguments against the law were rejected by a Clark County judge in 2015 and the city later lost an appeal. The city then appealed to the state supreme court. SOCIAL MEDIA: FOLLOW REPORTER MICHAEL COOPER ON&nbsp;FACEBOOK AND&nbsp;TWITTER. The city of Dayton&rsquo;s case was heard by the state&rsquo;s high justices earlier this year, which could affect if Springfield&rsquo;s case is heard or not. The red light cameras make the roads safer, Springfield resident Francine Goldberg said, but it hasn&rsquo;t changed the way she drives. &ldquo;We stop at the yellow (lights),&rdquo; she said. However, both Francine and her husband, Steve Goldberg, said they&rsquo;re not sure if the right person is getting fined, depending on who&rsquo;s driving the car. &ldquo;It might be someone else driving,&rdquo; Steve said. &ldquo;Maybe we ought to do away with the (red light cameras) and make the fine something stiffer if the police catch someone going through a red light.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local-govt--politics/springfield-won-follow-dayton-plans-keep-red-light-cameras-off/ezXk6xwYwnqlvIgUo6nFeJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/21/Videos/4042818.vpx\" title=\"Springfield won’t follow Dayton, plans to keep red light cameras off \" alt=\"Springfield won’t follow Dayton, plans to keep red light cameras off \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local-govt--politics/springfield-won-follow-dayton-plans-keep-red-light-cameras-off/ezXk6xwYwnqlvIgUo6nFeJ/\" target=\"\">Springfield won’t follow Dayton, plans to keep red light cameras off </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nMichael Cooper</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The city of Springfield won’t be resuming its red light camera program any time soon, despite Dayton’s proposal to turn its red-light and speed-detection cameras back on later this month to improve safety at intersections. \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Yo, Roger, they did it.&nbsp; When the NFL chose Philadelphia to host the 2017 draft, it quickly became clear one of the world's most famous movie locations would be the perfect site to hold the three-day extravaganza.&nbsp; &quot;We had talked about a couple different venues for the NFL and they wanted the Rocky steps and they wanted the Art Museum,&quot; Philadelphia Mayor Jim Kenney told The Associated Press. &quot;So that's what we gave them. It will be a terrific event.&quot;&nbsp; Perhaps NFL Commissioner Roger Goodell will run up the famous steps before announcing the No. 1 pick. Those same steps immortalized by fictional hero Rocky Balboa 41 years ago will serve as the backdrop for the elaborate stage, including a 3,000-seat, open-air theater, being constructed along the Benjamin Franklin Parkway for the NFL's first outdoor draft.&nbsp; The free event is being called the largest fan experience ever produced by the NFL. More than 200,000 people are expected to visit the half-mile area next week to enjoy the festivities. The first round begins Thursday night.&nbsp; &quot;There was no better way to highlight the city of Philadelphia than to showcase some of its most iconic venues &mdash; from the Benjamin Franklin Parkway, to the Philadelphia Museum of Art, to the Franklin Institute,&quot; NFL spokesman Kamran Mumtaz said.&nbsp; Some tourists and residents in the area have been grumbling for weeks about the lack of access to sidewalks and roads, and about the giant stage blocking most of the iconic &quot;Rocky&quot; steps.&nbsp; Visitors won't be able to run up those steps and raise their arms triumphantly at the top the way Sylvester Stallone did in the movie, but they could walk up the steps from the side and they can visit the Rocky statue at the bottom.&nbsp; Joaquim Marquet, of Valencia, Spain, came down to Philadelphia from New York City just to run up the steps Thursday.&nbsp; &quot;It was the one thing I wanted to do,&quot; the 32-year-old said. &quot;(It) was a bit disappointing, but I understand you can't stop everything just for tourists.&quot;&nbsp; There will be a ton of other, fun activities for visitors inside the NFL Draft Experience, which stretches the length of 25 football fields.&nbsp; One of the interactives includes an opportunity for fans to test their 40-yard dash time against players. Adults and kids can kick field goals, run through obstacles or try the new 100-yard zip line.&nbsp; &quot;I will not be on the zip line, but I'm sure the more adventurous will be lined up for it,&quot; Kenney said.&nbsp; Those who prefer to stay on the ground can get autographs from current and former players, take pictures with the Vince Lombardi Trophy and much more. Fans outside the theater can follow the draft on giant television screens.&nbsp; &quot;The NFL is really engaged in the fan experience process,&quot; said Julie Coker Graham, President and CEO of the Philadelphia Convention and Visitors Bureau.&nbsp; The City of Brotherly Love is getting used to major events. Philadelphia hosted the Democratic National Convention last summer and Pope Francis in 2015.&nbsp; &quot;We certainly do big events well,&quot; Coker Graham said. &quot;But to have the NFL choose us, our city, Philadelphia and then to choose the Benjamin Franklin Parkway, and the iconic steps of the Art Museum is kind of a dream come true.&quot;&nbsp; The 82nd NFL draft is returning to its birthplace for the first time since 1961 and 11th time overall. On February 8, 1936, the first NFL draft was held at Philadelphia's Ritz-Carlton Hotel. Teams took turns over nine rounds picking from 90 names written on a blackboard. There was no media coverage.&nbsp; Times have changed. This year's draft received its most fan interest since the event moved from New York in 2014. Chicago hosted the draft the past two years.&nbsp; Philadelphia fans, especially Eagles fans, are known for their boorish behavior. A group of Eagles fans who traveled to New York in 1999 welcomed No. 2 overall pick Donovan McNabb with a chorus of boos he never forgot. Surely, Goodell will be jeered.&nbsp; &quot;Our fans are very passionate and also very educated when it comes to sports,&quot; Coker Graham said. &quot;We support our players and teams and we share our opinions. We know company is coming, so we always put on our best smile when company is coming.&quot;&nbsp; The NFL says the event is expected to generate $80 million in economic impact for the city. Kenney didn't want to put a number on it.&nbsp; &quot;You can't put a price tag on the positive exposure the city gets,&quot; he said. &quot;You can't quantify the value of that.&quot;&nbsp; The full cost of the NFL draft is close to $25 million. The NFL will cover $20 million, and the remaining $5 million is coming from private funding secured by the city.&nbsp; &quot;The pope cost us $8 million and the NFL draft is costing us $500,000, so the ROI (return on investment) on that is quite spectacular,&quot; Kenney said.&nbsp; For Philly, the Hail Mary is greater than the Holy Father. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/sports/football/philly-set-welcome-nfl-draft-grand-way/Lvf9dRzJ8J8SXeK3FGGSeJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyAJC/2017/04/21/Images/venue%20AP.jpg\" title=\"Philly set to welcome NFL draft in grand way \" alt=\"Philly set to welcome NFL draft in grand way \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/sports/football/philly-set-welcome-nfl-draft-grand-way/Lvf9dRzJ8J8SXeK3FGGSeJ/\" target=\"\">Philly set to welcome NFL draft in grand way </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nRob Maaddi</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Yo, Roger, they did it. cacheScope type: class com.eidosmedia.portal.render.ctx.ContextMap -->\n        <!-- noRootContainer:  -->\n    \n \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n                        \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  A Stivers grad is gearing up to play what has been dubbed &ldquo;the most expensive flute in the world.&rdquo;&nbsp; Dayton native&nbsp;Brandon Patrick George, a New York based flutist, uses new and old instruments on an album that&nbsp;&ldquo;honors American craftsmanship and legacy.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wanted to challenge myself,&rdquo; George, who still has family members in friends in the Dayton area, told us. &ldquo;I thought it would be so much more fascinating to get different sounds out of different instruments.&rdquo; He is currently raising funds for his effort and recently wrapped up&nbsp;an&nbsp;Indiegogo campaign.&nbsp; Donors can receive&nbsp;CDs, performance tickets and other gifts based at different contribution levels.&nbsp; The yet-to-be named album will explore the concepts of forward and backwards thinking.&nbsp; &ldquo;I am exploring composers that were looking forward with their thinking or they are looking back,&rdquo; George said.&nbsp; One of the key instruments George will use on his musical journey is the platinum flute&nbsp;Verne Powell was commissioned to create for the 1939 World's Fair. Famed American flutist&nbsp;William Kincaid purchased the flute, which was under guard at the fair, according to&nbsp;flutebuilder.com. He left it to&nbsp;Elaine Shaffer, one of his favorite students, when he died. &nbsp; Following Shaffer&rsquo;s death at 47, her husband auctioned it off at Christie&rsquo;s in New York, where artist Andy Warhol was in attendance.&nbsp; A New York antique collector at that 1986 auction won the flute for $170,000, the equivalent of nearly $400,000 in today&rsquo;s money, &nbsp;according to most inflation calculators.&nbsp; The flute has a Dayton connection. George said Dayton native&nbsp;Phil Unger, the owner of the Flute Center of New York, supports the album&nbsp;project and is helping him secure use of the flute for the album from a private owner.&nbsp;&nbsp; Phil Unger and George met through Phil&rsquo;s father, the late&nbsp;Gilbert Unger. While at Stivers, George played a duet with the elder Unger, a clarinet player, at a dinner party.&nbsp; &nbsp;&ldquo;I made this connection with him,&rdquo; George said.&nbsp; George says the sound that comes out of platinum flutes is&nbsp;unique.&nbsp; &ldquo;It won&rsquo;t respond the same way as a silver flute,&rdquo; he said.&nbsp;&ldquo;You have to figure it out.&nbsp; Once you do, &nbsp;there are a lot of wonderful sounds you get out of it.&rdquo; The &nbsp;other instruments George intends to use includes a&nbsp; modern Verne Powell flute made from grenadilla wood. &ldquo; I wanted an album that I could get as many sounds that I could,&rdquo; he said. George, a 2004 Stivers School for the Arts graduate who first began playing the flute as a fifth-grader at Residence Park Elementary School, has an impressive resume built thinking out of the box. The Oberlin College Conservatory of Music grad has&nbsp;performed recitals and chamber music concerts at a list of concert halls that includes Alice Tully Hall, the Kennedy Center, Cit&eacute; de la Musique and Bargemusic.&nbsp; George has performed in the ensembles of Pittsburgh Symphony Orchestra and the International Contemporary Ensemble in Chicago. He gave props to his teacher at Stivers, his friends and a supportive mother.&nbsp; I had these dreams, but I had people that supported me,&rdquo; &nbsp;he said. &ldquo;It is those people, &nbsp;it is those voices &nbsp;that keep you going when it is hard.&nbsp;Of course it will be those that don&rsquo;t support you, but &nbsp;I don&rsquo;t think they matter that much.&rdquo; &nbsp; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/lifestyles/the-story-behind-how-this-stivers-grad-plans-play-the-world-most-expensive-flute/bnVKK6Gnym0DZL2wXaXDHP/\"
