href=\"/lifestyles/the-story-behind-how-this-stivers-grad-plans-play-the-world-most-expensive-flute/bnVKK6Gnym0DZL2wXaXDHP/\" target=\"\">The story behind how this Stivers grad plans to play the world’s most expensive flute&nbsp; </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nAmelia Robinson</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>A Stivers grad is gearing up to play what has been dubbed “the most expensive flute in the world.”  Dayton native Brandon Patrick George, a New York based flutist, uses new and old instruments on an album that “honors American craftsmanship and legacy.” “I wanted to challenge myself,” George, who still has family members in friends in the Dayton area...</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t            \n\t\t        \n            \n        \n    \n    \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n \n\n<!-- Rendering article, 41, false -->\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n        \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n            \n\t\t        \n\t\t            \n\t\t            \n\t\t                \n\t\t                    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\t                <!-- DEBUGGER: teasibleProperties.tag CUSTOM TEASE check -->\n        <!-- rootContainerSectionId: / -->\n        <!-- rootContainerPrimaryCategory: /Online/Home -->\n        <!-- object: PortalWebObjectWrapper [+     71956#] /Pub/p8/MyAJC/2017/04/20/Stories/042117 TRAVEL HARLEM.xml [ConcreteProxyImpl:EOM::CompoundStory, 2.0.3102576944] -->\n        <!-- cacheScope: ContextMap[_rootContainerContextMap:ContextMap[primaryCategory:/Online/Home, sectionId:/]] -->\n        <!-- cacheScope type: class com.eidosmedia.portal.render.ctx.ContextMap -->\n        <!-- noRootContainer:  -->\n    \n \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n                        \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n     \n\n\n        \n            \n            \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n\n\t            \n\t               \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Harlem has long had a romance with France. Well before the Harlem Renaissance of the 1920s, African-American artists and musicians traveled to France to broaden their artistic vision or to escape the daily oppression of American racism.&nbsp; Not widely known, however, is that the traffic went both ways, with French tourists visiting Harlem because of their fascination with jazz, gospel and black culture, even through the rough years of the 1970s and 1980s, when fear of crime kept away many Americans. During that era, my French came in handy more than once, giving directions to bewildered visitors.&nbsp;&nbsp; French-speaking Africans have settled and opened businesses on and around West 116th Street since the 1980s, with Petit Senegal lending the bustling thoroughfare a distinctly international air with passers-by in flowing boubous, shops selling phone cards for cheap calls to Africa, and Franco-African restaurants and vegetable stands offering tropical products like hot peppers, plantain and palm oil. But since the 1990s, a small French expat community, attracted by the romanticism of Harlem, its strong sense of community and colorful history, as well as by comparatively lower real estate prices, has sprung up, and, inevitably, so have French restaurants.&nbsp;&nbsp; Several restaurants are clustered around West 125th Street and Malcolm X Boulevard, still called Lenox Avenue by hard-core Harlemites and still the beating heart of Harlem, with one outlier on a corner of St. Nicholas Avenue that once hosted an unmemorable Chinese restaurant. As a Haitian-born immigrant who lived in Paris, Africa and for decades on Manhattan&rsquo;s Upper West Side before returning to Paris five years ago, I find the knowledge that I can eat a decent French meal on my trips back to New York &mdash; without traveling downtown &mdash; as comforting as a blanquette de veau on a crisp Paris evening.&nbsp;&nbsp; The presence of at least four traditional French restaurants in Harlem suggests in many ways how much Harlem has evolved, this neighborhood that has absorbed successive waves of immigrants, including the 17th-century Dutch farmers who named it after Haarlem back home. Then there were the Irish and Italians of the mid-19th century, and at the start of the 20th century, Jewish entrepreneurs and entertainers and African-Americans fleeing the segregated South. Now affluent millennials, including many whites, priced out of other parts of the city, have arrived.&nbsp;&nbsp; Harlem&rsquo;s embrace of the French restaurateurs has been warm. &ldquo;Harlem is a village,&rdquo; said Thierry Guizonne, the owner of Chez Lucienne. Increasingly, the village has a French accent.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For most of the 1950s and 1960s, the most visible Gallic presence in Harlem was Frenchy, the flamboyant Haitian-born Camillo Casimir, whose Casdulan Hairdressers on 125th Street was Harlem&rsquo;s largest beauty parlor. Frenchy traveled often to Paris to keep up with the latest styles and coifed the hair of Harlem VIPs, including Diahann Carroll and Mrs. Louis Armstrong.&nbsp;&nbsp; Today, in the rapidly gentrifying landscape that is Harlem, the French presence is best seen in four restaurants, in addition to African-owned places with classic French dishes on their menus alongside African specialties, including Patisserie des Ambassades on Frederick Douglass Boulevard and West 119th Street; Ponty Bistro Harlem on Adam Clayton Powell Jr. Boulevard at West 139th Street; and takeout sandwich shops like B&K French Cuisine on Adam Clayton Powell at West 128th.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Harlem, long known as the capital of black America, has a history as a culinary destination, primarily for soul food at longtime establishments such as Sylvia&rsquo;s on Malcolm X Boulevard near West 126th Street and Amy Ruth&rsquo;s on West 116th Street. On many evenings, busloads of European and Asian tourists can be seen jostling with the locals for an authentic experience of Southern cuisine. Long gone are Wells Supper Club, which allowed late-night revelers to have dinner and breakfast at the same time with its famous fried chicken and waffles, and Copeland&rsquo;s, which offered chitterlings and Champagne in Hamilton Heights and briefly on the Upper West Side before foundering in 2007.&nbsp;&nbsp; While the bulk of the media attention to Harlem&rsquo;s growing restaurant scene has lately gone to celebrity chef Marcus Samuelsson and his Red Rooster Harlem, launched in 2010 on Malcolm X Boulevard, with an eclectic mix of Scandinavian and soul food (both Swedish meatballs and fried chicken are on the menu), it&rsquo;s no longer difficult to find a decent coq au vin, a confit de canard or a boeuf bourguignon north of West 110th Street.&nbsp;&nbsp; Each of the four restaurants profiled here offers its own particular ambience.&nbsp;&nbsp; Barawine Harlem&nbsp;&nbsp; There are no berets or other French clich&eacute;s in sight at Barawine Harlem, on a corner of Malcolm X Boulevard at West 120th Street. Owned by Fabrice Warin, the restaurant and wine bar, with its subtle gray and brown color scheme, rows of wine bottles and subdued lighting, would fit right in with the new wave of cool branch&eacute; (plugged-in) bistros in the 10th Arrondissement around the Canal St. Martin. A long two-sided rectangular bar that doubles as a communal table takes up much of the space in the airy front of the house. Customers sit side by side or across from one another. Those who prefer conventional tables and larger groups can find them in the backroom, or, in good weather, on the sidewalk.&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; Warin (pronounced Vah-RHIN) is a native of Alsace who first moved to Australia, where he learned English, before finding his way to New York. He got a job as a waiter, studied to become a sommelier and worked for Alain Ducasse and Fran&ccedil;ois Payard before launching his own place.&nbsp;&nbsp; Warin, 44, has lived in Harlem since 2000 and had long dreamed of opening a French restaurant there. &ldquo;I have a passion for food and wine and Harlem,&rdquo; he said. But some investors remained wary of financing restaurants in the neighborhood until Samuelsson&rsquo;s Red Rooster opened a decade ago, he said. Gaetan Rousseau, a film producer and former neighbor in Harlem who had frequently heard his pitch, finally agreed to invest. Barawine is a Franglais word play on the French bar &agrave; vin.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Open since August 2013 for dinner daily and brunch on weekends, Barawine attracts a stylish and diverse crowd of young and old, black and white, neighborhood characters, tourists looking for a good meal and suburbanites who want to experience the &ldquo;new Harlem.&rdquo; &ldquo;We have a lot of French people on the weekend,&rdquo; Warin said, attributing the surge of European tourists in the past five years to an article in the French newspaper Le Figaro about black churches offering gospel music.&nbsp;&nbsp; The ambience at Barawine is cool and upscale, with a DJ spinning rap, hip-hop and soul at Sunday brunch, and live jazz Sunday nights and Tuesdays. A multiracial Francophone staff offers friendly and efficient service. Barawine features a standard French menu, including mussels, a charcuterie plate and hand-cut beef tartare. The food is tasty, well presented and reminds me of the bistronomique restaurants in the gentrifying neighborhoods of the 10th and 11th Arrondissements in Paris. The list is international, with 25 wines available by the glass at $9 to $15, and 200 wines available by the bottle. At dinner, first courses range from $10 to $16; main dishes, $17 to $36.&nbsp;&nbsp; Chez Lucienne Just a few blocks north, on Malcolm X Boulevard between West 125th and 126th Streets, is the grand-m&egrave;re of them all, Chez Lucienne, which opened at the end of 2008, two years before the Red Rooster arrived next door. Operated then by French restaurateur Alain Chevreux and named for his mother, Chez Lucienne changed hands in 2015. The owner is now Thierry Guizonne, 40, a native of the French Caribbean island of Guadeloupe, who had run a sushi restaurant in the Paris suburb of Rueil-Malmaison before moving to New York in 2014.&nbsp; Guizonne (pronounced Gee-ZONE) said many customers &mdash; unaware of France&rsquo;s increasingly diverse population &mdash; are surprised to see a black Frenchman as the owner. Explaining his origins can be difficult since, he said, most customers have never heard of Guadeloupe. &ldquo;I tell them it&rsquo;s near St. Martin,&rdquo; he said, referring to the popular Caribbean vacation destination.&nbsp; &nbsp;Guizonne said he benefits from the drawing power of the media-consuming Red Rooster just next door. &ldquo;I like to say people party at Red Rooster, but they come to my place to eat.&rdquo;&nbsp; His menu features many French classics, including, on a recent visit, an onion soup, a steak-frites and a cassoulet on a par with what I order in a standard neighborhood restaurant in my Paris neighborhood. At dinner, first courses are $8 to $16; main courses, $20 to $28. The atmosphere is vaguely colonial and a little faded, with exposed bricks, overhead fans, palms and a row of antique mirrors along one wall. Guizonne has plans for an upgrade of the d&eacute;cor and has opened an upstairs lounge with jazz and R&B music for dancing on weekends. He&rsquo;s also considering a new name. In good weather, his outdoor seating area is lively and ideal for checking out the boldface names going in and out of the Red Rooster.&nbsp;&nbsp; Ch&eacute;ri&nbsp;&nbsp; Ch&eacute;ri casts a different spell. In the middle of the block between West 121st and 122nd streets on Malcolm X Boulevard, it is tucked into a row of finely detailed brownstones, most with street-level commercial space, in a section of the boulevard that best displays the grandeur of old Harlem. Because churches and row houses dominate this part of Malcolm X instead of the monotonous high-rises farther north, the spaciousness of the thoroughfare can be fully appreciated here for its generous width and broad sidewalks.&nbsp;&nbsp; Ch&eacute;ri is a couple of steps down from the sidewalk on the ground floor of a brownstone, and the atmosphere is both casual and refined.&nbsp;&nbsp; &ldquo;I wanted people to feel they are in my living room,&rdquo; said Alain Eoche, 57, an energetic man who lives on two floors above the restaurant. He is a believer in the Chinese practice of feng shui and has organized and decorated the space himself. There&rsquo;s a grand piano, a bar along the left side of the room, a bookcase and a fireplace.&nbsp;&nbsp; The jewel of the location is the garden in the back, a covered space that can be opened to the sky in good weather. Ch&eacute;ri frequently features live music &mdash; a pianist most evenings, with a singer on occasion. The walls serve as a gallery for a rotating roster of French and Caribbean artists whom Eoche admires.&nbsp;&nbsp; Born in Nantes, on the Atlantic coast of France, Eoche (pronounced AY-osh) owned a restaurant in the chic Marais district of Paris for 20 years before moving to New York in 2013.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He opened Ch&eacute;ri in March 2014. The menu includes a merguez lamb burger, and something hard to find in France, a veggie burger. First courses are $9 to $21; main dishes, $19 to $27. Eoche does much of the cooking himself, including the daily special, and the food benefits from his personal attention &mdash; it is authentic and flavorful.&nbsp;&nbsp; Like the other three restaurateurs, he fell in love with Harlem&rsquo;s neighborliness. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re in New York, but not really,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;The village ambience makes immigrants feel more at ease.&rdquo;&nbsp;&nbsp; Maison Harlem&nbsp;&nbsp; Maison Harlem is several blocks west of the French cluster of restaurants around Malcolm X Boulevard and West 125th Street. On a corner of West 127th Street and St. Nicholas Avenue, it draws a more economically diverse crowd than the other three spots, from working-class African-Americans and Latinos to smartly dressed strivers and gentrifiers, all in a warm atmosphere that recalls a neighborhood bistro in a gritty section of Paris, like Belleville in the 20th Arrondissement.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The background is exposed brick, and the d&eacute;cor smacks more of accumulation than foresight &mdash; a gumball machine, an antique clock and assorted paintings. The bar, on the ground level of what used to be a Chinese restaurant, is lighted by tall windows during the day and is warm and cozy in the evening. It is the center of social interaction at Maison Harlem, with drinkers elbow to elbow on busy weekend nights and the overflow and those waiting for tables lined up along the narrow counter on the opposite wall.&nbsp;&nbsp; The large dining room is several steps up at the back of the bar, giving diners the sensation of stepping onto a stage. You have a choice of picking a table or a leather booth to monitor the bar scene or seats farther back for more privacy.&nbsp; Maison Harlem is nestled at the foot of the hill that leads to the City College campus and its hodgepodge of granite and white terra-cotta neo-Gothic buildings and stark modern structures. It draws a busy lunch crowd during the week, college faculty, students and employees of businesses around West 125th Street.&nbsp;&nbsp; The owner, Samuel Thiam, 45, is a native of Paris who grew up in the southern French city of Montpellier. An aunt and uncle ran a restaurant in the Paris suburbs. Thiam (pronounced TEA-am) came to New York as a dancer and actor, but a motorcycle accident ended that career. He got work as a floor director for television news shows. The hours were long, he recalled, and the job didn&rsquo;t fulfill him.&nbsp;&nbsp; The restaurant, now 4 years old, was a response to a personal need. &ldquo;I had a condo in Harlem and no bistros to hang out for a glass of wine,&rdquo; Thiam said. He found a business partner to finance the deal when a corner store became available. Reflecting a French &mdash; and New York &mdash; reality, location was important. &ldquo;You have to have a corner,&rdquo; he said. He has also opened a wine store directly across the street.&nbsp;&nbsp; The menu and the ambience echo his biracial origins &mdash; his mother was from Normandy and his father from the Ivory Coast. &ldquo;I wanted to have French food with an African panache,&rdquo; Thiam said. The background music leans toward Afrobeat and Nigerian music legend Fela Kuti, but you might hear French chansons at lunch and brunch. There are North African dishes, including a merguez sausage sandwich at lunch. First courses at dinner are $9 to $20; main courses, $14 to $32. He recently introduced a bar menu that offers sliders and oysters at a buck a piece.&nbsp;&nbsp; Thiam has been struck by how both old and new Harlemites are remarkably knowledgeable about French cuisine. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had so many people chatting with me about escargots, foie gras and p&acirc;t&eacute;s, it always surprises me,&rdquo; he said. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/travel/francaise-harlem/Lz7scG8GU11vvOUBqsAVEN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyAJC/2017/04/21/Images/HARLEM_FRENCH_RESTAURANTS_ADV16_7.JPG\" title=\"À la Française in Harlem \" alt=\"À la Française in Harlem \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/travel/francaise-harlem/Lz7scG8GU11vvOUBqsAVEN/\" target=\"\">À la Française in Harlem </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nJoel Dreyfuss</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Harlem has long had a romance with France. Well before the Harlem Renaissance of the 1920s, African-American artists and musicians traveled to France to broaden their artistic vision or to escape the daily oppression of American racism. Not widely known, however, is that the traffic went both ways, with French tourists visiting Harlem because of their fascination with jazz, gospel and black...</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t            \n\t\t        \n            \n        \n    \n    \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n \n\n<!-- Rendering noRootContainer:  -->\n    \n \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n                        \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n   \n\t               \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t    \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n arrested him this month on gun-related charges. &gt;&gt; Read more trending news&nbsp; Joshua Troutman, 17, was transferred from juvenile custody to the Palm Beach County Jail on charges that include possession of a weapon, discharging a firearm in public and grand theft of a firearm. He also has multiple open cases in juvenile court, including charges of carrying a concealed weapon, grand theft and burglary. During his initial court appearance on Friday, Judge Caroline Caroline Shepherd ordered Troutman&rsquo;s open juvenile cases be reviewed and set his bond on the adult charges at $50,000. He was also placed on in-house arrest with an electronic monitor and ordered to have possession of neither weapons nor devices that can access the internet. According to the arrest report, a Palm Beach County sheriff&rsquo;s detective last month monitored postings by Troutman on social-media sites, including Facebook and Instagram. The detective noted that Troutman posted several pictures and videos with multiple firearms in his waist, hands and pockets. Troutman is currently classified as a juvenile delinquent and is not allowed to possess firearms, investigators said. Authorities say he has a history of burglary and resisting arrest with violence. He remains under supervision as a juvenile until his 19th birthday. &ldquo;He is a danger to the community,&rdquo; a prosecutor said in court Friday. \n<!-- map content:  A video showing an apparent confrontation between a passenger and an&nbsp;American Airlines flight attendant has resulted in that attendant being removed from duty,&nbsp;WFAA The American team member has been removed from duty while we immediately investigate this incident. &quot;We are deeply sorry for the pain we have caused this passenger and her family and to any other customers affected by the incident,&quot; the airline said. He said his goal is to keep the city a great place to live work and play. &ldquo;Having four young children, I feel it is my duty as a councilman to preserve the quality of life for both my family and other Trojans,&rdquo; Twiss said.      \n\t                \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  The annual Big Lens Film Festival, which showcases the work of Wright State University&rsquo;s student filmmakers, will be held at the Rave Cinemas at The Greene in Beavercreek for the first time. This year&rsquo;s event, slated for 3-5 p.m. on Sunday, April 30, will be followed by a Q&A with the directors and a reception at Bar Louie, across the street from the theater. &ldquo;This year&rsquo;s batch of films is a very eclectic mix of genres featuring a macabre comedy, naturalistic dramas, a comedic web series and experimental narratives,&rdquo; says Steve Bognar, the Academy Award-nominated documentary film maker who teaches at Wright State and serves as advisor to this year&rsquo;s Big Lens festival. &ldquo;The students have been working on these films for over 18 months, and they are excited to be premiering them here in Dayton.&rdquo; Bognar says this showing will be the first time the student films will be seen in 2K resolution, using the same technology that regular Hollywood movies do. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s another big step forward for our Wright State film program, and it&rsquo;s thanks to the tireless efforts of our new production faculty member Lindsey Martin,&rdquo; he adds. The students whose work will be included are Victor Solo Greene (from Yellow Springs) and his film, &ldquo;Huella;&rdquo; Leah Byrd (from Trotwood) and her film, &ldquo;Hot & Bothered;&rdquo; Sean Mangan (from Xenia) and his film, &ldquo;Bucket;&rdquo; Christopher Blazavich (from Marietta) and his film, &ldquo;Angela Darling &rdquo; and Eric Dickey (from Newark) and his film, &ldquo;He didn&rsquo;t even leave a note.&rdquo; &lsquo;JAK&rdquo; was written and directed by Chloe Kristensen (from Waynesville) and a film entitled &ldquo;Louise&rdquo; was written by Haley Shepard and directed by Leighanna Hornick who is from Centerville. Topics range from the aftereffects of sexual assault to the loss of a family pet on a family. Bognar says his students work incredibly hard on these short films. &ldquo;They write, produce. shoot and edit the films,&rdquo; he explains. &ldquo;They fund all the movies themselves. It&rsquo;s a ton of work, a ton of collaboration, trial and error and dedication.&rdquo; Many of the filmmakers work on one another&rsquo;s films in a variety of capacities. After premiering at Big Lens, the films are sent out to film festivals around the country. Films from the program have been nominated for Student Academy Awards, Student Emmy&rsquo;s and have premiered at top film festivals, including Sundance. Advanced tickets can be purchased for $9 through the Wright State theater box office at www.wright.edu/theatre-dance-and-motion-pictures/performances/big-lens-film-festival. You can also call at (937) 775-2500. Day of show tickets are $10 cash at the door. &ldquo;Fantastic Brass&rdquo; at Art Institute The fourth and final concert in the Dayton Philharmonic Orchestra&rsquo;s 2016-2017 chamber series will take place at 3 p.m. Sunday, April 23, in the Dayton Art Institute Renaissance Auditorium. Conductor Patrick Reynolds and Dayton Philharmonic Orchestra Brass and Percussion musicians will present &ldquo;Fantastic Brass.&rdquo; The tradition of brass chamber music harkens back to Mozart&rsquo;s time. Selections from Giovanni Gabrieli, Anthony DiLorenzo, Philip Glass and Eckhard Kopetzki will be performed, along with works from Gustav Mahler and Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky. The afternoon will conclude with Percy Grainger&rsquo;s six-movement &rdquo; A Lincolnshire Posy,&rdquo; with each movement adapted from folk songs Grainger encountered on a trip to England in the early 1900s. Larry Coressel from Discover Classical WDPR 88.1 will give a pre-concert talk beginning at 2:30 p.m. Tickets are $22 for adults and $14 for students and children, available at the door. Humana Festival introduces new plays After introducing six world premieres to theater addicts and industry folks from around the nation over a six-week period, the 41st Humana Festival of New American Plays wrapped up in Louisville on April 9. This year&rsquo;s subjects ranged from a problematic wedding ceremony to nuclear proliferation and alien abductions. An audience favorite was &ldquo;Airness&rdquo; by New York playwright Chelsea Marcantel. The entertaining play transported audiences to the fascinating world of air guitar competitions. The show featured Matt Burns, the four-time reigning United States Air Guitar Champion. A touching and humorous drama, &ldquo;Cry It Out&rdquo; by Molly Smith Metzler, reflected the trials and isolation faced by two young mothers with newborns. Two women with local connections made their mark at this year&rsquo;s Festival as members of the prestigious Actors Theatre Professional Training Company, an intensive nine-month program. Alexis Breese, 23, has been interested in stage management since her days at Bellbrook High School. &ldquo;I like stage management because you&rsquo;re in the middle of everything and you&rsquo;re always solving problems,&rdquo; she told me. &rdquo; Your job is to make other people&rsquo;s jobs easier and you&rsquo;re always backstage during the show. Let&rsquo;s say there&rsquo;s an actor in one scene who has to appear in the next scene in an entirely different costume. My job is to help figure that out &mdash; how and when what change takes place.&rdquo; Breese, a 2016 graduate of the University of Michigan, plans to continue working in regional theater. &ldquo;I like being in a community,&rdquo; she explains. &ldquo;If you find the right group of people and the right community and a play that sparks what you love, you really can create something incredible!&rdquo; Anne-Marie Trabolski, who grew up in Centerville, was an acting member of the training company and landed a starring role in the Humana Festival&rsquo;s play, &rdquo; We&rsquo;re Gonna Be Okay.&rdquo; A graduate of the Savannah College of Art and Design, she says the apprenticeship was worth every &ldquo;hard, long day.&rdquo; Now headed back to New York to work as an actor, Anne-Marie says the people she met in Louisville will always be part of her life. &ldquo;I learned so much about myself as a person and artist and can&rsquo;t wait to kick-start my acting career,&rdquo; she says. Steinberg Awards announced The Humana Festival weekend traditionally concludes with the announcement of the winners of the American Theatre Critics Association Steinberg Awards for new plays. At $40,000, Steinberg/ATCA is the largest national new play award program recognizing regional theaters as the crucible for new plays in the United States. Michael Crisofer&rsquo;s &ldquo;Man in the Ring&rdquo; was the winner of the 2016 Harold and Mimi Steinberg/American Theatre Critics Association New Play Award, recognizing playwrights for scripts that premiered professionally outside New York City during 2016. Based on the true story of a boxer who killed a man during a bout, &ldquo;Man in the Ring&rdquo; premiered at the Court Theatre in Chicago. Two citations were presented to Tracy Letts&rsquo; &ldquo;Mary Page Marlowe&rdquo; and David Rabe&rsquo;s &ldquo;Visiting Edna,&rdquo; both of which premiered at Steppenwolf in Chicago. Don&rsquo;t be surprised to see this winning plays show up on Dayton stages in the next few years. Nature Literature Trail opens Love this idea! In an effort to expose children to nature and literature at the same time, the folks at Fairborn Parks & Recreation have joined with the Fairborn Library to publish &ldquo;Hawk&rsquo;s Woodland Walk,&rdquo; a nature literature trail that displays consecutive storyboards along Valle View Reserve&rsquo;s walking path, located off Commerce Center north of East Dayton-Yellow Springs Road. The author of the story is Ann Cooper, head librarian at Fairborn&rsquo;s Community Library. An kick-off event for the project will be held at 10 a.m. at Valley View Reserve, 1097 Empire Court, located behind 5/3 Bank off Commerce Center Blvd. There will be a 10:30 a.m. live hawk presentation by a Five Rivers MetroPark naturalist and storybook reading, crafts, games and refreshments from 11 a.m. until noon. For more information, call (937) 754-3090. Music Men to present Veterans Concert The Miami Valley Music Men, a barbershop chorus that performs in and around the Miami Valley, will present its annual &ldquo;A Salute to Our Veterans&rdquo; concert at 3 p.m. Saturday, April 29. Special guest is Dulahan, a group that plays contemporary Celtic music. The show will take place at Beavercreek Church of the Nazarene, 1850 N. Fairfield Road in Beavercreek. &ldquo;All proceeds from this event are donated to the Paralyzed Veterans of America Buckeye Chapter,&rdquo; says the group&rsquo;s vice president of marketing Bill Gruenwald. &ldquo;Last year, we donated $2,200.&rdquo; Tickets to the event can be purchased at the door for $10. Children ages 12 and under are $5. The chorus has also been selected to sing the National Anthem each year at a Dayton Dragons Game so you can expect to see them on the field. For more information, check out www.singdayton.org. Astronomy Day at Boonshoft Museum &ldquo;Bringing Astronomy to the People&rdquo; is the theme of Astronomy Day at the Boonshoft Museum of Discovery slated for noon to 4 p.m. Saturday, April 29. Folks from around the globe will have an opportunity to experience the fun of astronomy by observing the sun, moon and stars and visiting displays provided by amateur and professional astronomers. &ldquo;Whether museum-goers want to know more about complicated phenomena like black holes and the formation of stars, or they simply want to know what to look for in the sky, people continue to be fascinated by astronomy,&rdquo; says Cheri Adams, director of astronomy. The museum is teaming up with the Miami Valley Astronomical Society (MVAS) to celebrate the special day with safe solar observing (weather permitting) and astronomy demonstrations. Guests interested in astronomy can learn the proper use of telescopes, ask questions of astronomers, and participate in special activities. Admission to Astronomy Day activities is free for members and included with paid admission to the Boonshoft Museum of Discovery for non-members. Admission is $14.50 adults, $12.50 seniors (60+), and $11.50 for children (3-17). Children under 3 and members are free. (937) 275-7431. BoonshoftMuseum.org Opera Ball planned The Opera Guild of Dayton will present its annual Opera Ball, &ldquo;Night of a Hundred Stars,&rdquo; on Saturday, April 29 at the University of Dayton&rsquo;s River Campus, 1700 S. Patterson Blvd. The special evening, chaired by Opera Guild president Penny Wolff, will feature fine dining, dancing to Bob Gray&rsquo;s orchestra and entertainment. Tickets for the black-tie optional gala are $175 - $275 per couple. First-time attendees can arrange a table for eight for $1,400. For reservations, call Sue Falter at (937) 429-2944. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/entertainment/arts--theater/big-lens-film-fest-showcases-wright-state-students/PxtrpGO7qoHkT9kSHnK3gN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyDaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18350296_Huella.jpg\" title=\"Big Lens Film Fest showcases Wright State students \" alt=\"Big Lens Film Fest showcases Wright State students \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/entertainment/arts--theater/big-lens-film-fest-showcases-wright-state-students/PxtrpGO7qoHkT9kSHnK3gN/\" target=\"\">Big Lens Film Fest showcases Wright State students </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nMeredith Moss, <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The annual Big Lens Film Festival, which showcases the work of Wright State University’s student filmmakers, will be held at the Rave Cinemas at The Greene in Beavercreek for the first time. \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Mike Defibaugh is living his dream. The 43-year-old Bellefontaine native and resident not only fished in his first professional walleye tournament April 12-13, but he won it. And with that, he announced he would be turning over all $15,000 of his cash winnings to the Make-A-Wish Foundation, which grants requests to children diagnosed with life-threatening diseases. &ldquo;To be fishing as a pro on the tournament circuit is a dream come true for me,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;My wife (Mandy) and I talked about it when we decided I should turn pro and we decided we would donate any cash I earned this year. And since I am living my dream, we figured we would help kids have a chance to live their dreams.&rdquo; The tournament that he won was the Cabela&rsquo;s National Walleye Tour season-opening event out of Huron on Lake Erie. In addition to his $15,000 cash, first prize included a new $46,000 Ranger boat and Evinrude motor. Defibaugh&rsquo;s knowledge of Lake Erie paid off on the second and final day of the tournament. As the boats headed out of the Huron River, almost all turned left to find some of the large spawning walleyes off of the Camp Perry reefs &mdash; quite a long haul from Huron. Instead, Defibaugh turned right and fished close to shore, 5-8 miles east of Huron. &ldquo;The lake was muddy and the wind was blowing,&rdquo; Defibaugh recalled. &ldquo;So I looked for places with better water clarity. I think I ended up just five miles from the ramp.&rdquo; Defibaugh was using deep-diving Bandits with planer boards. His color combinations varied, but a chartreuse head, purple sides and a pink belly worked best. He said the more important adjustment was adding Pro-Cure, a gel scent, on the top of each crankbait. &ldquo;I think the scent was key to locating the crankbaits,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;They were hitting it hard; the boards would just rocket back. There was no mistaking there was a fish on.&rdquo; He finished with 64.65 pounds, 42.26 coming on the second day when he rose from 22nd place after the first day with 22.23 pounds. &ldquo;I went west to the reefs the first day and caught one good fish (out of the five he weighed). With the way the wind was blowing on the second day, I decided to stay closer to home.&rdquo; The second-place finisher was Ryan Buddie of North Ridgeville, Ohio at 62.61 pounds. Mike Robertson of Laura came in fourth with 58.53 pounds and won $16,568. Defibaugh, who is a mechanic for CSX Railroad, credits his wife and sponsors (Ranger, Knox Marine, Indian Lake Bass Pro and Reef Runner Lures) for giving him his chance on the pro circuit. &ldquo;I am lucky my wife and kids (three) have been behind me all the way. We talked about turning pro all winter,&rdquo; said Defibaugh. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been blessed with a job that keeps all the bills paid with a little left over to put gas in the boat and buy a few worms. And since I was able to get some sponsors, we decided to give it a try.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/sports/bellefontaine-man-makes-dreams-come-true/tt4anDSt6Va8kAOBG6fNAI/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyDaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18349539_Defibaugh.jpg\" title=\"Bellefontaine man makes dreams come true \" alt=\"Bellefontaine man makes dreams come true \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/sports/bellefontaine-man-makes-dreams-come-true/tt4anDSt6Va8kAOBG6fNAI/\" target=\"\">Bellefontaine man makes dreams come true </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nJim Morris, <span class=\"story-source\">Contributing Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Mike Defibaugh is living his dream. single pin to qualifying for the Pepsi State Tournament, Julia South has come a long way in a short time. The 6-year-old Kettering girl got her start on the lanes just over a year ago in the Kids Bowl Free program and recently qualified to the state event. She and her brother, Griffin, 8, both competed in the Pepsi District Tournament. &ldquo;She was very excited about qualifying to state,&rdquo; Julia&rsquo;s dad, Andrew South, said. &ldquo;She has improved so much.&rdquo; Julia now regularly breaks 100 and her big brother &mdash; who also got his start in Kids Bowl Free &mdash; usually breaks the 100-pin mark in two of three league games. Kids Bowl Free is a summer program that has introduced the sport to millions. Last year, more than 2.3 million kids from across the country and Canada took advantage of the program. Participation is easy as parents can register their children &mdash; 15 and younger in most cases &ndash; at www.kidsbowlfree.com. Parents can select the most convenient bowling center and, once the registration is complete, will receive weekly coupons via email for two free games daily. Participation days and hours are determined by the individual centers and are available online. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t expect them to take to it the way they did, but I&rsquo;m glad they enjoy it,&rdquo; Andrew said. &ldquo;Anything that keeps the kids moving is a good thing, but what&rsquo;s great about bowling is you can do it year-round whether it&rsquo;s hot or cold outside.&rdquo; And, with bowling, fun and fitness can go hand-in-hand &mdash; a plus for parents trying to make sure their children stay active. According to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, children and adolescents should participate in at least 60 minutes of physical activity a day. Bowling is one way to meet that requirement. And, did you know that two games of bowling engage as many as 184 muscles? It can also help improve coordination and balance. But it&rsquo;s fun, not fitness, that keeps Julia on the lanes. &ldquo;My favorite thing is getting strikes and spares,&rdquo; she said with a smile. The dates for the Kids Bowl Free program vary by center with most running from about mid-May through late July or early August. But Julia and Griffin weren&rsquo;t about to call it quits after the program ended last summer as they joined a Saturday youth league at Poelking Woodman Lanes.        \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Christopher Durang&rsquo;s 2013 Tony Award-winning comedy &ldquo;Vanya and Sonia and Masha and Spike,&rdquo; a kooky yet touching look at a dysfunctional family, will be presented by Beavercreek Community Theatre beginning Friday, April 28. Set in a farmhouse in Bucks County, Pennsylvania, the delightful, zany tale concerns the strife between the introverted Vanya and his more outspoken sisters Sonia and Masha. As the feuding trio attempts reconciliation, a sassy cleaning lady with a penchant for voodoo, a goofy hunk, and an aspiring actress heighten the play&rsquo;s quirkiness, an essential element at the center of many works by Durang. In fact, a Snow White-themed costume party prominently factors into the action here. &ldquo;I really like Durang&rsquo;s somewhat weird brand of humor, but (this play) seems to me more mainstream,&rdquo; said Chuck Larkowski, who portrays Vanya. &ldquo;Yet there are still those off-the-wall moments. There aren&rsquo;t a lot of one-liner jokes in this play. The humor really grows out of the characters for the most part, or at least that&rsquo;s true for Vanya, who is more of a reactor to his more vocal sisters. There are long stretches in which he says very little, which of course makes his eventual outburst all the more effective.&rdquo; Vanya&rsquo;s terrific outburst, an undeniable Act Two highlight and one of the best monologues written in recent years, occurs when Vanya catches Spike, Masha&rsquo;s narcissistic boy-toy, texting during a play he wrote. His rant humorously details a simpler time when typewriters, Ed Sullivan, the Mickey Mouse Club, and non-self-adhesive postage stamps brought America together. &ldquo;The monologue was a bear to learn because the sequence of ideas is so random and stream-of-consciousness,&rdquo; Larkowski admits. &ldquo;But in spite of its seeming chaos, this monologue gives us a vivid snapshot of Vanya and his postmodern angst.&rdquo; &ldquo;The script is extremely well written,&rdquo; added director Doug Lloyd, who staged the local premiere of &ldquo;Eating Raoul&rdquo; earlier this season for BCT. &ldquo;The characters are funny and each one is flawed in their own quirky way. Although they are flawed, each one grows in enormous ways by the end of the show. You can&rsquo;t help but love them. This is a hilarious show with a lot of heart.&rdquo; &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t stop laughing the first time I read the script,&rdquo; echoed Ryan Petrie, fully prepared to strip down for the majority of the play as the uninhibitedly sexual Spike. &ldquo;I grew up on a lake so I was always shirtless. I&rsquo;ve become desensitized to other people seeing me in very little to no clothes. I&rsquo;m definitely not shy.&rdquo; The cast includes Debra Kent as Sonia, Cassandra Engber as Masha, Cassidy Manley as Nina, and Erin McGee as Cassandra. Chris Harmon notably provides scenic design. The best place to find what to do, what know and what to love about Dayton. In addition, Durang pays homage to the sensibilities of Russian playwright Anton Chekhov (1860-1904). In fact, the names Vanya and Sonia are taken from &ldquo;Uncle Vanya,&rdquo; Masha from &ldquo;Three Sisters,&rdquo; and Nina from &ldquo;The Seagull.&rdquo; Also, Masha, a self-absorbed actress, is colorfully akin to Arkadina in &ldquo;The Seagull.&rdquo; &ldquo;I love the Chekhov references and themes that run throughout the play,&rdquo; said Kent who recently staged the local premiere of &ldquo;Luna Gale&rdquo; for Dayton Theatre Guild. &ldquo;Vanya is resistant to change, Sonia lives with a great deal of regret, and Masha worries about getting older and keeping up with a world that is moving too fast. What resonates for me is the loneliness of the two sisters and brother, and how they&rsquo;ve blamed others, and each other, for their non-accomplishments and unhappiness through the years. It&rsquo;s not occurred to them all these years that they are responsible for their own happiness, and that state of mind is entirely up to the individual. (This play) is funny in a quiet way, but can still pull a huge laugh from the audience.&rdquo; Durang&rsquo;s works include &ldquo;Betty&rsquo;s Summer Vacation,&rdquo; &ldquo;Beyond Therapy,&rdquo; &ldquo;The Marriage of Bette and Boo,&rdquo; and &ldquo;Mrs. Bob Cratchit&rsquo;s Wild Christmas Binge.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/events/performing-arts/beavercreek-presents-quirky-vanya/mLa4SqsecwGYUT9aRUk8XK/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18475593_vanya.jpg\" title=\"Beavercreek presents quirky ‘Vanya’ \" alt=\"Beavercreek presents quirky ‘Vanya’ \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/events/performing-arts/beavercreek-presents-quirky-vanya/mLa4SqsecwGYUT9aRUk8XK/\" target=\"\">Beavercreek presents quirky ‘Vanya’ </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nRussell Florence Jr., <span class=\"story-source\">Contributing Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Christopher Durang’s 2013 Tony Award-winning comedy “Vanya and Sonia and Masha and Spike,” a kooky yet touching look at a dysfunctional family, will be presented by Beavercreek Community Theatre beginning Friday, April 28. pulled over a car that had caught his attention by traveling 104 miles per hour long after midnight, just south of Kingman. He smelled marijuana in the car. It was driven by a man with an adult female wearing only lingerie. Their passenger was a female juvenile whose fake document showed her to be 18. She was, Otto says, &ldquo;not wearing a whole lot of clothing.&rdquo; The adults had taken this 16-year-old from California to Arizona and were heading for Las Vegas. The girl gave Otto the California phone number of her grandmother, who immediately told him the girl might have been in prostitution since she was 15. Trained in interview techniques for such situations, and experienced at noticing people who somehow do not belong together, Otto correctly suspected DMST &mdash; domestic minor sex trafficking. Trooper Mitch Jergenson, 46, stopped a car driven by a man whose passenger was a 17-year-old girl he had gotten to know via Facebook and other social media. He had paid for her ticket from California to Phoenix and was taking her to Las Vegas. She said she was going to be a &ldquo;model,&rdquo; then said she was going to work in a strip club. This, says Jergenson, is &ldquo;the start of a process&rdquo; whereby minors often wind up working the streets. Sgt. Scott Reutter, 47, who watches the motels near the Phoenix intersection of I-17 and I-10, where prostitutes are active, approached a young girl talking to an older man. She said she was 22. Reutter, whose daughters are 22 and 19, thought she was &ldquo;14, maybe 15.&rdquo; She had been a runaway for 17 months, since she was 13, and said that if she were returned to the custody of child services she would run again. After a 10-minute hearing, she was returned. She immediately ran, and did so repeatedly. To be in law enforcement is often to feel condemned to bailing an ocean with a thimble. Frank Milstead, too, knows how Sisyphus felt. When nature designed him, it had a director of the Department of Public Safety in mind. Large and laconic, he is the 54-year-old son of a Phoenix cop and he knows in the marrow of his bones that &ldquo;there are so many people out there who want to take advantage of other people.&rdquo; It is unclear how many victims of DMST there are because for many reasons the crime is not often reported by its victims. They are, Milstead says, usually abducted, sort of, from &ldquo;some environment where nobody missed them,&rdquo; adding, however, that traffickers cannot control &ldquo;people who are unwilling.&rdquo; But many trafficked minors, &ldquo;who no one had made to feel valuable,&rdquo; are, Milstead says, &ldquo;chronic runaways&rdquo; with attenuated capacities for self-determination. Milstead&rsquo;s troopers patrol motel parking lots and get to know those who do the motels&rsquo; housekeeping and notice suspicious activity. Troopers also watch bars and nightclubs where minors are offered for sex, and, increasingly, monitor the internet and social media. The website Backpage, whose founders live in Arizona, began as a place for normal classified advertising but, a U.S. Senate investigation concluded, found its most lucrative business being a sexual marketplace. The New York Times reports that law enforcement officials say Backpage&rsquo;s &ldquo;dating section&rdquo; often &ldquo;used teasers like &lsquo;Amber alert&rsquo; and &lsquo;Lolita&rsquo; to signal that children were for sale.&rdquo; Holding up his smartphone, Milstead, whose vocation reinforces his inclination to look on the dark side, says: &ldquo;Leaving your kid alone at night in his room with this? You might as well leave him or her in the city park downtown. Twp., this brick-and-vinyl two-story has an extended floor plan that allows for open social area. Listed for $189,900 by Irongate Inc. Realtors, the home at 2292 Polo Park Drive has about 2,200 square feet of living space plus a finished basement. Dark hardwood flooring fills the entryway of the home and wraps around the formal living room into the center of the home where the family room, breakfast room and kitchen are all open to each other. The extended kitchen and breakfast room add to the openness of the living space. Along one wall are cherry cabinets with granite countertops surrounding stainless-steel appliances. A window is above the double sink and a double-door panty closet is near the arched walkway into the formal dining room, which has a tray ceiling. A glass door between the kitchen and the breakfast room opens out to a wood deck, which has steps that lead down to a smaller wood deck and the fenced back yard. The semi-private back yard is deep and has a storage shed. The home&rsquo;s breakfast room has a cathedral ceiling and several windows that allows plenty of natural light into the breakfast room and filters into the family room. Tucked into a corner of the family room is a gas fireplace with fluted wood mantel and ceramic-tile surround and hearth. Off the family room through an arched walkway are a laundry room equipped with a washer and dryer, a half bathroom and access to the two-car garage. A door off the family room leads to the finished basement. Most of the basement is one large recreation room with overhead and sconce lights and carpeting. There is a closet under the staircase and a finished bonus room, which is large enough for a hobby or office area. Unfinished space has the home&rsquo;s mechanical systems. The second level features four bedrooms and two full bathrooms. The main bedroom has a cathedral ceiling with ceiling paddle fan. The bathroom is divided with a dressing area that has a double-sink vanity and walk-in closet. The other half has a walk-in shower with glass doors. Three other bedrooms have ceiling paddle fans and double sliding-door closets. The guest bath features a whirlpool tub/shower and extended-counter vanity with single sink. MIAMI TWP. noRootContainer:  -->\n    \n \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n                        \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n     \n\n\n        \n            \n            \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n\n\t            \n\t               \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Many of us were first introduced to the &ldquo;Violins of Hope&rdquo; when the historic instruments were featured on the popular television show, &ldquo;CBS Sunday Morning.&rdquo; The 20 restored violins, which continue to be exhibited and played throughout the world, once belonged to victims of the Holocaust. The true story that accompanies each instrument is a testament to the horrors of Nazi Germany, to the power of music and to the survival of the human spirit. Those of us who saw the exhibit at Cleveland&rsquo;s Maltz Museum learned that for Jews facing unbelievable evil, the music of a violin could offer refuge, hope and give expression to their In some instances, the ability to play the violin helped save lives. Thanks to the generosity of local survivor Bob Kahn, the Miami Valley has its own violin treasure. The Butler Twp. man&rsquo;s beloved violin &mdash; and his story &mdash; are on display at the National Museum of the U.S. Air Force as part of an exhibition entitled &ldquo; Prejudice and Memory.&rdquo; RELATED: Holocaust survivor&rsquo;s forgiveness mission earns Indiana honor At 93, Robert Kahn is one of only a handful of survivors still living in Dayton. He has just published his autobiography and will share reflections as the featured speaker at the Greater Dayton Yom Hashoah Remembrance today &mdash; Sunday, April 23 &mdash; at Temple Beth Or in Kettering. The annual event free and is open to the public. &ldquo;There will come a time when all of the survivors of the Holocaust are gone,&rdquo; Kahn says. &ldquo;We need to make sure that their memories will continue.&rdquo; A conversation with Bob Kahn By the age of 15, Kahn &mdash; who was living with his family in Mannheim, Germany &mdash; had already been stripped of many aspects of everyday life &mdash; his school, his friends, the pleasures of walking, swimming, playing outside and all forms of entertainment. &ldquo;We were practically under house arrest,&rdquo; he remembers. &ldquo;My leisure time was confined to my stamp collection, reading and having a Jewish musician come to our house to teach me how to play the violin. It was one of the things Hitler had not been able to control when our economic and social life had been curtailed by law.&rdquo; Q. How did that violin come to play such a significant role in your life? A. In 1938 on Nov. 9th and 10th &mdash; known as the &lsquo;Night of the Broken Glass&rsquo; &mdash; all of the Jewish homes and 1,200 synagogues were destroyed. Our home was ransacked by the Nazis &mdash; everything in it was thrown from our balcony and set on fire. My father was beaten with clubs and I was ordered by the S.S. to play my violin for the amusement of the crowd that was watching below. They were all laughing and enjoying themselves. That night, my father was taken away to Dachau, a concentration camp. One of the few things left in our home was my violin. I lovingly covered it with a red bedspread that the Nazis had overlooked and hid it in the attic. Years later, when I came back as an American soldier after World War II, I contacted our former janitor from the apartment building and inquired whether he had found a violin in the attic. Weeks later a package arrived in Dayton with my violin still wrapped in the red bedspread. RELATED: Artwork Nazis stole in WWII returning to Jewish owner&rsquo;s heir I placed in in our attic above the garage. For many years, no one knew that my precious violin was there. Q. When did you decide to take the violin out of the attic and share your story? A. I haven&rsquo;t played a violin since that day in 1938. I just couldn&rsquo;t. But when I joined Dayton&rsquo;s Holocaust Education Committee, I took the violin to show the group. After that, it became the centerpiece of talks I began to give at schools and on other occasions. When the Air Force Museum decided to install an exhibit dealing with the Holocaust, I decided to give my violin a meaningful resting place where its story could be permanently displayed. Q.\n            I understand you will be speaking on a different subject at this year&rsquo;s Holocaust memorial? A. Yes, I fought with the U.S. Army in the Pacific. In 1946 I was living in Chicago and saw a newspaper ad asking for people who could speak and understand technical German. I got the job and came to Dayton to work for &ldquo;Project Paperclip,&rdquo; a top secret program conducted by a U.S. intelligence agency at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base. The purpose was to gain military advantages over the Soviet Union in the Cold War, and the space race. Approximately 80 German aeronautical experts were brought to the base and they were interrogated about their contributions to German Air Force developments, production, related weapons and technology. Their background was never revealed but we assumed that most of them were Nazis. That posed difficulties because of my background. It was difficult for me to have anything but a professional relationship with them. READ NOW: Germany approves bill curbing online hate crime, fake news They didn&rsquo;t talk about it but through documents and microfilm, I saw evidence of the cruel tests performed on human being by Nazi scientists, engineers and doctors. The general public is unaware of the ways in which these helpless individuals died. It is those heroic Jews who were ruthlessly murdered that I want to memorialize. Q.\n            Tell us about your book and why you wrote it. A. My children and grandchildren constantly ask me questions about my life. Over the years, I only gave them fragmentary replies. I realized that in order to satisfy their curiosity I needed to write a detailed autobiography &mdash; about my ancestors, my parents and sister, my young life and the 18 relatives who lost their lives in the Holocaust. I wanted to write about my escape to freedom, my life in America and service as an American solider, my work with Army intelligence. I wrote about my marriage to my wife, Gert &mdash; who I met at a USO gathering where she was a volunteer &mdash; about my children and grandchildren. My first great-grandchild was born two weeks ago. Q.\n            The title of your book is &ldquo;The Hard Road of Dreams.&rdquo; What does that mean to you? A. I was always dreaming about a wonderful life. In retrospect I came to realize that I was leading a wonderful life. But the road to get there was fraught with all sorts of tragic events. The subtitle is &ldquo;Remembering Not to Forget.&rdquo; Q. What lessons can others learn from your story? Your violin? A. I hope that they can learn to resist any indifference to anti-semitism and other forms of religious and racial hatred. This is especially important for young people because they will become the caretakers of our nation. They can use their voices to reject evil. They will have an impact on their world and can help minimize, if not curtail, the slaughter of innocent people in other parts of the world. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/events/performing-arts/survivor-bob-kahn-will-speak-holocaust-memorial-event/UK9UxURcy1dz2IByj0iEfJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyDaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18209670_IMG_3930.jpg\" title=\"Survivor Bob Kahn will speak at Holocaust memorial event \" alt=\"Survivor Bob Kahn will speak at Holocaust memorial event \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/events/performing-arts/survivor-bob-kahn-will-speak-holocaust-memorial-event/UK9UxURcy1dz2IByj0iEfJ/\" target=\"\">Survivor Bob Kahn will speak at Holocaust memorial event </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nMeredith Moss, <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Many of us were first introduced to the “Violins of Hope” when the historic instruments were featured on the popular television show, “CBS Sunday Morning.” The 20 restored violins, which continue to be exhibited and played throughout the world, once belonged to victims of the Holocaust. The true story that accompanies each instrument is a testament to the horrors of Nazi Germany...</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t            \n\t\t        \n            \n \n<!-- map content:  Earlier this month, Anna Gros of Fairborn was driving home when she turned onto Interstate 675 and hit a large piece of concrete sticking out of a pothole. The large piece of debris blew out a back tire on the car, causing her to lose control and swerve into another lane on the highway. &ldquo;I was able to control my car. I was blessed,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;ve ever blown out your tire, you know it&rsquo;s really hard to control it.&rdquo; The damage to her vehicle &mdash; including a destroyed rim, tire and strut &mdash; cost more than $590. Potholes cost drivers, local municipalities and the state billions of dollars each year in damage repairs, insurance claims and civil complaints. The state has already used 8,818 tons of pavement patching material to repair potholes this year alone. That&rsquo;s up from the 6,823 tons last year and 4,178 tons used in 2015. Matt Bruning, spokesman for the Ohio Department of Transportation, said the cost of fixing each pothole varies &mdash; it could be hundreds of dollars to thousands of dollars. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll notice that we&rsquo;ve used a lot more material patching potholes over the past couple of years,&rdquo; Bruning said. &ldquo;While we&rsquo;re seeing more potholes, we&rsquo;re also doing a better job fixing them as quickly as we can.&rdquo; Gros said the pothole was fixed the next day after her accident, and that the crews had responded quickly and appropriately. The state has already spent $4.06 million to fill potholes. The department does not track data on how many potholes are repaired each year. &ldquo;If it is a more permanent fix, you will have a bigger crew, more equipment, more expensive asphalt,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;In the permanent fix you will cut out the damaged material. You make the cuts just outside of the damaged area, so that you get a good bond when you put in the new asphalt.&rdquo; Civil complaints can be filed against the state in the Ohio Court of Claims for pothole damage. If the pothole damage is $10,000 or less, drivers can file a complaint with the state court that was established in 1975 by the General Assembly. More than 1,000 Ohioans filed complaints in the past five years and were reimbursed nearly a half million dollars by the state. More than 125 civil complaints have been filed for vehicle damage because of potholes this year, according to a state database. That number is slightly down from a couple years ago when claims skyrocketed after a brutally cold winter with quickly swinging temperatures. Approximately 100 civil complaints related to potholes were filed in 2013, and that number spiked to 355 filed in 2015. Locally, some say the unseasonably warm winter conditions resulted in fewer potholes and damage vehicles &mdash; costing local municipalities and the state less in road repair and civil claims. Fred Stovall, public works director for Dayton, said there&rsquo;s been less this year &mdash; similar to trends seen with last year&rsquo;s mild winter as well. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re always going to have potholes,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a mild winter, and we do have some pot holes but it&rsquo;s nothing out of the norm.&rdquo; Potholes are typically caused by weather patterns. Through the winter months, water gets under the pavement and frees, then thaws, repeatedly. That expansion and contraction of water causes roadway material to break up. That action mixed with heavy traffic results in potholes. &ldquo;But potholes aren&rsquo;t just a winter problem,&rdquo; Bruning said. &ldquo;They can happen in warmer weather too. Water gets under the pavement, breaks down the asphalt and creates a void. Then the traffic on top creates depressions in the pavement.&rdquo; Potholes are most noticeable when the temperature starts to warm up again after cold weather, and crews usually have the busiest work time in January and February, according to the transportation department. Mark Breining, manager at Grismer Tire in Dayton, said it&rsquo;s been a slow year for vehicle damage due to potholes. The mild winter has kept more cars on the streets and out of auto repairs shops, he said. &ldquo;The snow was so minimal,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Everyone got a break from it this year.&rdquo; If a vehicle does hit a pothole, it can do extensive damage and cost upwards of hundreds of dollars for repair. A patch repair cost about $25, but a new wheel could cost up to $300 plus the price of a new tire. If the alignment or suspension of the car has been impacted, that will cost even more. While municipalities say there&rsquo;s not many ways to prevent potholes, one local company is working to analyze and disseminate pothole data. Founded by James Bridgers, Road-Aid uses geospatial and sensor, automatic target recognition on vehicles to identify where potholes are at &mdash; and then collect, analyze and inventory data about road issues. Bridgers said the goal is to better help consumers to understand where potholes are at, he said. And, governments can more readily &ldquo;deploy a fully proactive pothole identification program&rdquo; if they have the data to use. &ldquo;The average driver spends about $800 bucks a year just in vehicle maintenance due to poor road conditions- whether it&rsquo;s flat tires due to potholes or just normal bumpy roads,&rdquo; he told this newspaper. &ldquo;As they are driving along they will be notified and then actually have the image of where that pothole is so they can drive defensively and avoid it safety.&rdquo; &bull;&nbsp;Kroger to create 600 jobs with new Dayton-area stores &bull;&nbsp;RETAIL ROUND-UP: 3 stories about Cabela&rsquo;s, RadioShack, Starbucks &bull;&nbsp;Development officials see growth in downtown &bull;&nbsp;Centerville strip mall demolished next to Elsa&rsquo;s &bull;&nbsp;Ohio economy to win big with future NCAA games -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/business/potholes-costly-for-state-motorists/4VpDeKbdR1OJ85noNIaLKJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18460386_IMG_0873.jpg\" title=\"Potholes costly for state, motorists\" alt=\"Potholes costly for state, motorists\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/business/potholes-costly-for-state-motorists/4VpDeKbdR1OJ85noNIaLKJ/\" target=\"\">Potholes costly for state, motorists</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nKara Driscoll</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word;    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t
