 \n    \n<!-- map content:  The former editor of a once-prominent Turkish newspaper is worried the rich, diverse country at the crossroads of Europe and Asia is drifting toward an autocratic ruler with little regard for preserving freedoms of expression &ndash; including a free press &ndash; conditions that allowed Turkey to flourish as a secular democracy. &ldquo;I am a witness and a victim of Turkey&rsquo;s break from democracy,&rdquo; said Abdulhamit Bilici, a journalist for 25 years. Now a political refugee in the United States, Bilici led Zaman Daily, which had been the largest newspaper in Turkey until it was seized and eventually shut down by the party of Turkish President Recep Tayyip Erdogan. Bilici will be in Dayton on May 1 at the invitation of the Dayton Council on World Affairs. He will speak about the fast-moving changes in Turkey, including crackdowns on journalists. The 5:30 p.m. event will be hosted by Cox Media Group Ohio, publisher of this newspaper. The importance of Turkey to the United States and international interests could not be greater, Bilici said. An ally of the U.S. for 60 years, Turkey is the only Muslim country in NATO and has NATO&rsquo;s second-largest Army in terms of troop numbers. The country also sits near Russia, Iran, Syria and Iraq in a volatile, strategic region. &ldquo;Now there is a big chance that the West is losing Turkey &hellip; and Turkey is getting each day closer to Russia and other axes,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Keeping Turkey as a democratic country is a very important strategic issue for Turkish people and the friends of Turkish people.&rdquo; Carolyn Rice, an executive board member Dayton Council on World Affairs, heard Bilici speak in Washington, D.C. last November. She said the newsman has a compelling story. &ldquo;I would think our community would want to know Turkey is an important ally and an important country in all that&rsquo;s happening,&rdquo; said Rice, who is also Montgomery County treasurer. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s not coming to make a political speech or be talking against Erdogan. It is truly about the topic of the importance of a free press in a democratic society.&rdquo; Bilici described Zaman Daily&rsquo;s editorial stance as conservative-progressive, a sober-eyed, middle-of-the road approach for the region&rsquo;s only Muslim democracy. &ldquo;We were supporting family values and we were respecting religious values and traditional values of Turkish society. This was our conservative aspect,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;But under the progressive aspect, we were defending the rule of law and democracy and Turkey joining the European Union. So it was a combination of both sides.&rdquo; The Istanbul-based paper first supported Erdogan&rsquo;s efforts as a prime minister pushing democratic ideals and inclusion in the European Union, Bilici said. But the paper&rsquo;s editorial stance shifted as Erdogan&rsquo;s party pressured, purged or jailed protesters, journalists and prosecutors as well as others the ruling AKP party saw as threats through a 2013 corruption scandal. Erdogan&rsquo;s government labeled the paper, among others, a terrorist organization. On March 4, 2016, troops stormed the building and took over the presses. Bilici left Turkey about 15 days later. Speaking by phone last week, he said family members are with him in the U.S. but declined to discuss where they are living due to security concerns. Bilici said none of the paper&rsquo;s columnists&rsquo; views &ndash; whether nationalist, conservative, progressive or leftist &ndash; could find favor with Erdogan&rsquo;s government. Fifty journalists from the paper &ndash; including those who worked to objectively cover the news &ndash; are currently jailed, he said. At least 81 journalists were imprisoned in Turkey as of Dec. 1, according to the Committee to Protect Journalists. Two years after Erdogan became president, a military faction attempted a 2016 coup, and the government declared a national emergency. Bilici said resulting restrictions made work even more difficult for even-handed newsgatherers. &ldquo;The failed coup last July was also an important last turning point that he started to rule the country by emergency that is now continuing,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Erdogan is using that as an opportunity to silence any position, leftist, liberal or conservative, nationalist, whatever.&rdquo; Erdogan now controls 90 percent of the Turkish media, Bilici argues, making it easier to consolidate power in elections such as one a week ago that could result in abolishment of the country&rsquo;s existing parliamentary system of government. He also said he detects a similarity in the way some in the U.S. media have been treated at the White House following the election of President Donald Trump, when some reporters were barred from press briefings. &ldquo;There are some parallels, but of course America has a much stronger tradition of democracy and very important, strong institutions,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;There is a common understanding in the United States to defend freedom of expression, although those newspapers and media don&rsquo;t agree on political issues.&rdquo; Bilici said in just five short years Turkey&rsquo;s democracy was &ldquo;hijacked&rdquo; by Erdogan&rsquo;s party, resulting in a muzzled press and judiciary. He hopes those parallels don&rsquo;t stretch too far. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t mean that America will start jailing its journalists &ndash; I hope it doesn&rsquo;t go that far &ndash; but the people should be ready to defend democracy by defending freedom of expression and an independent judiciary,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Because when you lose those two, you don&rsquo;t have a chance to defend democracy. These are red lines.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story \">\n\t    <div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a League Baseball statistics, studies show that a 29-year-old centerfielder saves an average of four fewer runs per season than a 28-year-old centerfielder, The New York Times reported this week. While you may wonder why a prestigious newspaper would use valuable space to report stuff like that, baseball fans take such statistics seriously. And no statistic is too trivial to report. Somewhere in baseball&rsquo;s huge trove of baseball records, there may be one for &ldquo;Most errors committed by a left-handed shortstop born in Alaska.&rdquo; (This is a hyperbolic example. Only 11 of the 18,672 players in the history of baseball was born in Alaska, none of whom was a left-handed shortstop.) Talking about statistics gives fans something to do when nothing&rsquo;s happening on the field, which is most of the time. In an average three-hour game, according to one study, there are nine minutes and 45 seconds of actual activity. Unless you count players jogging to and from their dugouts as actual activity. MORE D.L.: Just &lsquo;plane&rsquo; carried away at the doctor&rsquo;s office. Now that most baseball fans in the stands are armed with cellphones, they don&rsquo;t have to wait to get home to their computers to savor the thrill of baseball statistics. Instead of watching a hitter stand in the batter&rsquo;s box and not swing at a pitch, they can Google the record for standing in a batter&rsquo;s box and not swinging at a pitch. Which, of course, was set last season by Bryce Harper of the Washington Nationals, who imitated a statue for seven consecutive at bats in a 13-inning game. Devout fans can use authentic statistics such as the following to engage in meaningful dialogues. Fan No. 1: &ldquo;Did you know Felix Hernandez is the only pitcher who made 16 consecutive starts, pitched at least seven innings and gave up two or fewer runs?&rdquo; Fan No. 2: &ldquo;Duh. But did you know that three pitchers who started 13 games and lasted fewer than seven games allowed at least three runs?&rdquo; READ MORE D.L.: A lifelong goal is inching away. The downside about getting engrossed in these statistics at the ballpark is that fans may not pay attention to the 9:45 of action on the field. In 1957, Philadelphia&rsquo;s Richie Ashburn set the record for most foul balls hitting the same fan (2); the first one struck spectator Alice Roth in the face, breaking her nose. As they carted her away for medical attention, Ashburn hit another foul ball, which struck the unfortunate Ms. Roth in the leg, breaking a bone in her knee. My guess is that she had been distracted by talking to the fan next to her about Cleveland&rsquo;s Bob Feller, who, in 1939, set the record for throwing the most pitches that were fouled into the stands, striking the pitcher&rsquo;s mother on Mother&rsquo;s Day (1). -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story \">\n\t    <div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/lifestyles/stewart-enjoying-major-league-baseball-the-numbers/foz6LoPk3sMmGBVlnQO3YK/\" target=\"\">D.L.        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  &ldquo;Salt Fat Acid Heat &mdash; Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; by Samin Nosrat (Simon and Schuster, 462 pages, $35). Hundreds of cookbooks are published each year. Some are good. Others are e,xceptional. A few are essential. Samin Nosrat just published &ldquo;Salt Fat Acid Heat &mdash; Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; and I daresay this one is essential. Cookbooks are laden with recipes &mdash; with that information we should be able to make dishes turn out well, right? The efforts we put into transforming recipes into flavorful dishes can sometimes feel like a waste of time. Foods that looked wonderful on the page don&rsquo;t always turn out as we might have expected. It really helps if we actually understand how to cook. That&rsquo;s the fabulous thing about this book &mdash; it teaches readers about cooking, how to employ various techniques, and how to grasp that any subtle variations in technique can have significant impacts upon our end results. It is possible to learn how to cook great food. As a child, Nosrat enjoyed the Persian dishes her family prepared. While attending college she had a meal at what is considered to be the best restaurant in America, Chez Panisse. That experience changed her life. She applied for a job there. As she observed and participated in the cooking taking place, she had a revelation. In the introduction to her book she reveals her realization that &ldquo;anyone can cook anything and make it delicious.&rdquo; MORE BOOK NOOK: A dozen great reasons to discover this series She explains, &ldquo;there are only four basic factors that determine how good your food will taste: salt, which enhances flavor; fat, which amplifies flavor and makes appealing textures possible; acid, which brightens and balances; and heat, which ultimately determines the texture of the food.&rdquo; After we fully comprehend that salt, fat, acid and heat are &ldquo;the four cardinal directions of cooking,&rdquo; we are ready to transform recipes and/or products of our own imaginations into appetizing dishes. We can build the confidence to cook on the fly while using the best ingredients available at the time, to improvise, to experiment, to really savor our flavor adventures. She goes into depth in each section &mdash; we delve into the science of what she is doing. For example, we learn about &ldquo;the Maillard reaction, heat&rsquo;s most significant contribution to flavor.&rdquo; When we employ acids in cooking the Maillard reaction can produce sour flavors. When we are using heat it will create savory scents and new textures. &ldquo;Salt Fat Acid Heat &mdash; Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; contains an abundance of material written in an approachable manner while remaining informative. Oh, and it is also filled with tempting recipes. Now that I feel hopeful that I have absorbed her food science and techniques, I&rsquo;m ready to embark on culinary expeditions. First I&rsquo;ll try out her recipe for slow-roasted salmon. This book is bound to become an indispensable addition to cookbook shelves throughout America.   \n<!-- map content:  So it turns out Alex Jones was only kidding. That time the radio host and ringmaster of the &ldquo;Infowars&rdquo; website said the government brought Ebola into the country to terrorize us? That time he said a Beyonce video was created to start a new civil war, that time he wished gang rape on Jennifer Lopez, those times he suggested the Oklahoma City bombing, the Sept. 11 attacks and the bombing of the Boston Marathon were &ldquo;false flag&rdquo; operations by the government against the people? It was all an act. His lawyer says he was just playing a character. You see, he&rsquo;s a &ldquo;performance artist.&rdquo; That claim, reported Sunday by the Austin American-Statesman, came in an Austin courtroom as Jones&rsquo; ex-wife, Kelly, seeks custody of their three children on the not-unreasonable grounds that a man who spews spittle for a living is not someone you want raising your kids. She says Jones is &ldquo;not a stable person.&rdquo; But lawyer Randall Wilhite argued that judging Jones by his on-air persona would be like judging Jack Nicholson by his performance as The Joker in &ldquo;Batman.&rdquo; In other words, like the Oscar-winning actor, Jones is just pretending to be a madman. You have to wonder how Edgar Welch feels about that. He&rsquo;s the North Carolina man who shot up a pizzeria in Washington, D.C., last year because he believed a tale spread by Jones and others that it was the headquarters of a child molestation ring run by Hillary Clinton. He faces the possibility of many years in prison when he is sentenced in June. You have to wonder how Leonard Pozner feels about it, too. He&rsquo;s been getting death threats and has been challenged to prove that his son Noah ever existed, all because Jones and others claimed the 2012 shooting at Sandy Hook Elementary School, in which Noah and 25 others were killed, was a hoax. He faces the rest of his life without his child. Finally, you have to wonder how Donald Trump feels about it. The so-called president has professed admiration for Jones and has built his worldview, such as it is, around a Jones-like belief that a tangled skein of conspiracies explains virtually everything in life that refutes, frustrates or embarrasses him. We face four years of him steering the ship of state. As regards the lawyer&rsquo;s claim, there are two possible conclusions. One: he&rsquo;s telling the truth and Jones never believed the garbage he vomited. Or two: Jones is trying to hoax the court. Not that it matters which is true. Either way, Jones has hurt people and ruined lives. Either way, he has helped damage the country. We now live in the United States of Confusion, a nation of alternative realities and alternative facts where reasoned and informed political debate is all but impossible because too many of us prize ideology above factuality. A coterie of media charlatans eagerly caters to that intellectual flaccidity and Jones was loud among them, so there is a certain satisfaction in seeing him revealed as a hypocrite and fraud. But the feeling is fleeting. After all, given the gullibility of his followers, there is no reason to believe this will be the end of him &mdash; or what he represents. Jones fills a need. Frightened people seek easy ways to comprehend the big, bad world. Alternative facts and realities are among the easiest. And never mind the damage that is done, the ignorance that is fostered, the pain that is caused. Meantime the rest of us &mdash; dare we still say, &ldquo;most&rdquo; of us? &mdash; muddle through actual reality using actual facts to confront the big, bad world. It is not easy. But it sure beats the alternatives. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story \">\n\t    <div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/opinion/opinion-turns-out-alex-jones-was-only-pretending-madman/upsnqXPzylC0067oqTFcaI/\" target=\"\">Opinion: Turns out Alex Jones was only pretending to be a madman </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nLeonard Pitts, <span class=\"story-source\">Miami Herald</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>So it turns out Alex Jones was only kidding. \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n     \n\n\n        \n            \n            \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n\n\t            \n\t               \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  The first time she saw the photo of the missing brown-eyed girl it mesmerized her. That was five years ago and it&rsquo;s no different today. &ldquo;Her name is Sierra LaMar and she went missing relatively close to where I grew up in California,&rdquo; University of Dayton rower Rosario &ldquo;Rosie&rdquo; Perez said Friday afternoon. &ldquo;She was in high school &mdash; a kid about my age then &mdash; and she just disappeared. &ldquo;The first time I saw the missing girl poster our (high school) team was going to a lacrosse tournament in Gilroy. The poster was at the entrance to the complex and her story just really hit me. After that I followed the &lsquo;Find Sierra LaMar&rsquo; Facebook page and everything. It was just so sad. And she&rsquo;s still missing today. They have no idea what happened to her body.&rdquo; LaMar &mdash; a 15-year-old sophomore cheerleader at nearby Sobrato High School who soon became known as &ldquo;Everyone&rsquo;s Daughter&rdquo; &mdash; went missing on a March morning in 2012 as she headed to the bus stop to go to school. A few months later, a 21-year-old high school dropout was arrested and charged with her murder. He denies involvement and his lawyers have dragged the case out for years. The trial is going on right now. &ldquo;That story really moved her,&rdquo; Rosie&rsquo;s mom, Monica Perez Burnett, said the other day. &ldquo;The image of that girl really stayed with her. She was like &lsquo;Gosh, something needs to be done about these types of things.&rsquo; And that has prompted her to want to go into the type career she&rsquo;s pursuing right now. She wants to make a difference.&rdquo; Now a senior rower for the Flyers who is about to graduate with a double-major degree in criminal justice and psychology, Rosie Perez hopes to do just that. So does her roommate and best friend, Danielle Foust, also a graduating senior, a standout on the rowing team and a criminal justice major as well. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t have the typical college experience and I&rsquo;m thankful for that,&rdquo; said Foust, who could just as easily have been speaking for Perez. Both of the 22-year-olds are top students and college athletes and in their four years at UD, one or the other has done everything from don a bullet-proof vest for a U.S. Marshals operation here to help in the investigation of a West Side homicide with the Dayton police to be part of drug raids and federal probation visits. Perez and Foust, both of whom are headed into law enforcement careers after they graduate from UD in two weeks, already have been immersed in the field with a variety of hands-on internships during their college years. Foust &mdash; who has interned with the district attorney&rsquo;s office in Salt Lake City, Utah, Catholic Social Services and both the United States Probation Office and the U.S. Marshals Service in Dayton &mdash; has gone through a series of extensive applications and upon six months of academy training in Georgia and Maryland, is headed to a federal law enforcement position in Washington D.C. Perez, who has prepped with various parts of the Dayton Police Department and has passed some prelim tests, hopes to join the Dayton police force or another in Ohio in the fall. She has gotten some real on-the-job training, be it working next to Chief Richard Biehl or in the field with a cold-case detective she especially admires. Along the way she has ended up various police situations, including the investigation of a homicide last summer. &ldquo;It was my first dead body,&rdquo; she said quietly. &ldquo;What affected me most was the state of the house and the little kids who were living there. &ldquo;A middle-aged guy was shot by his girlfriend and they had three young children &mdash; 6 and under &mdash; who were living in horrible conditions. It was really sad for them. Now their dad was gone and their mom was going to jail for his murder. &ldquo;The youngest kid had a mental disability and he was asleep on the couch the entire time. When he woke up, he was really confused by it all. &ldquo;What really hit me was the little girl. She was playing with My Little Pony. And at that time my youngest sister was obsessed by My Little Pony. &ldquo;They were probably the same age. I looked at that girl and thought she could have been my sister. Here they were, two little girls in love with the same thing, but their circumstances were so incredibly different. &ldquo;It just makes you want to do all you can for people in situations like that.&rdquo; Perez grew up mostly in East Palo Alto, California, which, she said, had serious crime and poverty issues when she was born in 1995. Three years earlier it had the highest homicide rate in the nation according to a report in The Stanford Daily. In recent years the crime numbers for the city &mdash; whose population is mainly Hispanic, but also has many African-Americans and Pacific Islanders &mdash; has subsided dramatically and there have been an influx of workers from nearby Google, Facebook and Amazon operations. &ldquo;When I was growing up there were drugs and gangs and drive-by shootings in our neighborhood, but I really knew nothing about that,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;My parents and my grandparents, who we lived with, were very protective and they raised us pretty well. We weren&rsquo;t involved in anything bad. &ldquo;I ended up at a private girls&rsquo; school because my mom taught music there. My direct family did really well actually. One of my cousins followed me here to Dayton, another graduated from Yale and another went to Harvard.&rdquo; When it came time to pick a college, she said she wanted to move away and looked at schools like Marquette and Creighton before choosing UD without ever setting foot on the campus. Her first day here was &ldquo;move-in day&rdquo; for freshmen. Foust came to UD by a bit of happenstance, as well. Growing up in Rives Junction, Michigan near Jackson, she looked at some in-state schools and then considered Bowling Green until someone suggested she look at UD if she was going to matriculate at an Ohio school. She said she &ldquo;fell in love with the campus&rdquo; and once here she heard about the rowing program, which recruits most of its athletes with what Coach Mike Wenker called its &ldquo;dorm storm.&rdquo; Members of the team canvas the dorms asking students if they&rsquo;d be interested in trying out for the (non-scholarship) rowing team. They also put up posters and pass out handbills and that invitation intrigued Foust, who had been a three-sport athlete &mdash; basketball, soccer, cross country &mdash; in high school. &ldquo;But I couldn&rsquo;t ever get her to go kayaking with me,&rdquo; laughed her mom, Kris Foust. The UD pitch struck a chord though, especially because prospects need not have rowed before. &ldquo;Rowing is a weird sport,&rdquo; Wenker said with a laugh. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s one of the only sports you can start as an 18-year-old and maybe end up on the national team.&rdquo; Case in point was former UD rower Bernadette Marten. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s the only rower in the (UD) Hall of Fame and after she graduated from college (Michigan), she ended up a world champion,&rdquo; Wenker said. Foust became one of the team captains this season and Perez had been an integral part in their eight-woman boat until she tore ligaments in her wrist a few weeks ago in the weight room. She&rsquo;s also a budding power lifter who recently won her first competition Along with representing the university in intercollegiate competition &mdash; the team competed in Pittsburgh on Saturday, is in Virginia today and the Atlantic 10 Championships are in New Jersey in two weeks &mdash; both women say the lessons and demands of the rowing team serve them well in other parts of their lives, including their law enforcement endeavors. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s pretty demanding,&rdquo; Kris Foust said of her daughter&rsquo;s commitment to the sport. &ldquo;They get up or 4 or 5 in the morning to train (on the Great Miami River) and then they go to classes and then get together for another workout later.&rdquo; She said it has helped Danielle become really regimented and disciplined. But it&rsquo;s not for everyone, said Danielle: &ldquo;We started out with 25 girls freshman year and now we&rsquo;re graduating with five. You really have to be committed. You&rsquo;ve got to be able to work out every day and balance your time. You&rsquo;re going to class and a lot of the girls are working and interning while rowing, too. And through it all you have to enjoy what you&rsquo;re doing and that can be a very thin line.&rdquo; The lessons, though, are invaluable, she said: &ldquo;I work out every day with a group of girls who have the same goals as me, the same passion as me. And that translates now to (law enforcement) where we work together &hellip; to better the community.&rdquo; Perez sees a correlation of what happens in the boat to what happens in life: &ldquo;With rowing, whatever you do, big or small, is going to affect every single person in the boat. Like if you are early or late (with your stroke) or your hand handle height is a little low or high, you&rsquo;re gonna affect the next person, good or bad. And all that can be implemented into daily life. &ldquo;Everything you do &mdash; a kind gesture or something not so nice &mdash; is going to affect someone else.&rdquo; Kris Foust graduated from Michigan State with a criminal justice degree and before raising her family with husband Dan, she worked for the Michigan Supreme Court. When Danielle took up the same course of study, Kris said she suggested her daughter &ldquo;do several internships to find out what you like. See if you want to go in enforcement or social work or administration.&rdquo; Danielle did that and said she settled on enforcement as a way you might be able to bring about &ldquo;a positive experience in someone else&rsquo;s life.&rdquo; Rosie agreed and said she especially has her old neighborhood in mind: &ldquo;Seeing things that happened there and the way some of the younger generation views the police made me want to do this all the more for those people in the rough environment. I think with my background &mdash; I&rsquo;m Hispanic &mdash; I can connect two worlds together.&rdquo; Her mom &mdash; who has remarried, moved to North Carolina and now has eight kids total, including two sets of twins, ages 6 and 3 &mdash; said she takes special pride in her oldest daughter. She said the other day someone from UD contacted her to put together some thoughts on Rosie for an upcoming rowing banquet. &ldquo;The words that came to mind were strength &mdash; she is extremely strong physically and mentally &mdash; and commitment. She&rsquo;s a person of her word. People believe her. They trust her. When she says something, when she starts something, she sticks with it. &ldquo;I admire her for that. I tend to start something and then easily step out of it when it gets boring. Then I move on to the next thing. Not her. She doesn&rsquo;t waver.&rdquo; Kris had similar praise for her daughter: &ldquo;Danielle is the oldest of our four kids. She&rsquo;s got a younger sister at Bowling Green, a brother at Grand Valley (State) and another who&rsquo;s a junior in high school. She&rsquo;s been a great leader for them. She&rsquo;s shown you need to put yourself out there &mdash; sometimes in an uncomfortable situation &mdash; and stand on what you believe. She&rsquo;s taught them that when you&rsquo;re at college, make the most of it. Do the very best you can. Do what you can to make a difference.&rdquo; That&rsquo;s the same idea Rosie Perez had when she saw that missing girl poster several years ago and, in the words of her mom, said &ldquo;something needs to be done.&rdquo; Now she&rsquo;s doing it. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/sports/tom-archdeacon-rowers-determined-make-difference/y8bgMFFnNLGaxRhSrRZ6YK/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/20/Videos/4038704.vpx\" title=\"Tom Archdeacon: UD rowers determined to make a difference \" alt=\"Tom Archdeacon: UD rowers determined to make a difference \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/sports/tom-archdeacon-rowers-determined-make-difference/y8bgMFFnNLGaxRhSrRZ6YK/\" target=\"\">Tom Archdeacon: UD rowers determined to make a difference </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nTom Archdeacon</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The first time she saw the photo of the missing brown-eyed girl it mesmerized her. service day Friday, aiming to teach students the value of helping others in their community. Students from the elementary school up to the high school took Friday to serve the community. Depending on the student&rsquo;s age, they tackled tasks like planting flowers around the school, visiting a local nursing home, washing cars, painting lockers, picking up trash and other activities that made St. Paris and the surrounding area a better place than how they found it. Middle school student Max Barela said the day was fun and taught him and his classmates a lot. &ldquo;We are building a flower garden for our school,&rdquo; he said of the task he was assigned. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s pretty cool, it&rsquo;s a good way to get to know everybody and do some teamwork.&rdquo; &ldquo;The community service is also great,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;It really helps the kids understand how helpful you can really be if you put some time and effort into this stuff.&rdquo; School staff members said they were proud of students who worked hard throughout the day to help their community. Graham Director of Operations Don Burley said the students embraced the day. &ldquo;There were core teams of student leaders that planned this day,&rdquo; Burley said. &ldquo;They set up all the events and made the contacts. They coordinated with teachers and staff on who will be overseeing the different projects and those student leaders created an environment of service and selflessness.&rdquo; The students are a great example for others, he said. Staff member Emily Smith said the event is part of the school&rsquo;s strategic plan to get students involved in the community and doing community service. &ldquo;Graham is very involved in providing service opportunities for our students,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve worked for several months now to make the events today possible.&rdquo; Smith coordinated the event and said it was great to work with such determined students. Multiple companies from the area donated money, items and time to make service day a success. Burley said he expects the service day to become a tradition at the school district. &ldquo;This has been a great first attempt and this is something that Graham is going to do every year,&rdquo; Burley said. &ldquo;We want to create great citizens for our community. of being a great vaulter. The Wayne High School senior is strong, has sprinter speed and a can-do attitude, all things needed to stick a pole in the ground and launch yourself skyward upside down. She also has something else going for her this spring. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s super mental and me, I&rsquo;m also a super mental person,&rdquo; Graysen explained after soaring a meet-best 12 feet in the 67th Dayton Edwin C. Moses Relays at Welcome Stadium on Friday. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve learned you just have to adapt to the situation you&rsquo;re in. That&rsquo;s my motto for the season, adapt. I&rsquo;m struggling but I&rsquo;m getting through the season with it. We&rsquo;re getting there.&rdquo; Dunbar needed to be six points better than Thomas Worthington in the meet&rsquo;s final event, the 4x400-meter relay, to win the boys championship. The Wolverines placed second in the event (3:29.77) and Worthington was fifth. That was a four-point spread, which enabled Worthington to win the boys team title, 63-62 over Dunbar. Trotwood-Madison (59.5) was third, followed by Wayne (56) and Northmont (50.5). Troy (80.5) won the girls team title, beating runner-up Worthington (63.5), Lima Senior (62), Centerville (61) and Northmont (59.5). Simmons is among another outstanding bunch of boys and girls vaulters this spring. Vandalia-Butler freshman Dalton Shepler cleared 14 feet to lead the boys individuals. All the events were running relays and two-man field event totals. &ldquo;It means a lot because I&rsquo;ve been struggling the past few meets getting over 12 and getting new sticks,&rdquo; said Simmons, who missed three times in what would have been an outdoor personal best of 12-6. &ldquo;I finally hit my big pole and I blew through my last jump. Now, we&rsquo;re going on my next stick. I&rsquo;m finally over 12 (feet) and I&rsquo;m super excited.&rdquo; Simmons said vaulters are a unique group who often share the same advice from coaches and train together in the offseason. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s something in the water,&rdquo; said Simmons, who hopes to continue vaulting at Eastern Michigan University. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re all friends. The vaulters are one big family. That&rsquo;s what I love about it.&rdquo; &bull; The meet also featured a unique Swedish Sprint Medley Relay: The opening leg was a 100, followed in order by a 200, 300 and 400. \n<!-- map content:  Wayne Asher is ready for his Edgewood High School softball team to make the jump from solid to strong. He believes the Cougars are starting to come together as they&rsquo;ve won five of their last six games, including Friday&rsquo;s 13-1, six-inning overpowering of host Talawanda. &ldquo;I think we&rsquo;ve been playing at about 70 percent,&rdquo; said Asher, who briefly retired after his 16th season at the helm last year, then opted to return in 2017. &ldquo;We had a good gap in the team earlier in the year. Once we close the gap all the way &mdash; and it will happen &mdash; then I&rsquo;ll feel really good about this team.&rdquo; Junior pitcher Sam Davis agreed that team chemistry was an issue when the season began. &ldquo;Usually this team is really close and like sisters, but we didn&rsquo;t really hang out at the beginning of the season,&rdquo; Davis said. &ldquo;Then when we were not doing so hot, we did a team bonding and started picking each other up and started winning. I think we&rsquo;re finally starting to jell.&rdquo; &ldquo;I think it was just different ages,&rdquo; said Asher, noting that he has five seniors and a starting lineup that includes three freshmen. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had chemistry problems before where the girls just weren&rsquo;t nice to each other. These girls aren&rsquo;t mean to each other. They just need to come together a little more.&rdquo; Davis led the way against Talawanda, tossing a four-hitter with seven strikeouts while going 4-for-5 with three RBIs. Edgewood is 8-4 overall and 4-1 in the Southwest Ohio Conference, and all four of the Cougars&rsquo; defeats have come by one run. Little Miami (7-2) is the only two-loss squad in SWOC play. &ldquo;We could easily be undefeated right now, but we&rsquo;ve had some bad breaks here and there,&rdquo; Asher said. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m looking for us to get on a roll like we did last year.&rdquo; The Braves (5-10, 2-5) didn&rsquo;t challenge Edgewood. The hosts avoided the shutout when Lauren Baker, who had two hits, delivered a two-out RBI single in the sixth. Talawanda committed eight errors along the way, seven in the last three innings. &ldquo;They brought their &lsquo;A&rsquo; game and we didn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; Braves coach Stephanie Guenther said. &ldquo;I know at one point Sam Davis had six strikeouts in nine batters. She&rsquo;s a good pitcher, but we need to at least be swinging the bat. We just looked panicky on defense, and it went downhill really quick.&rdquo; Madison Asher had three hits and three RBIs for the Cougars, and Jenna Evans added two hits and an RBI. Ally Williams, Hailey Jackson and Michaela Finkbine drove in a run apiece. Davis issued no walks and pushed her record to 8-3. She&rsquo;s on the mend from a groin injury. &ldquo;The good thing is we kind of jumped on them in the first inning with two runs,&rdquo; Wayne Asher said. &ldquo;With Sammi pitching, that&rsquo;s a pretty good start.&rdquo; He said Davis was one of several EHS players that convinced him to coach this year. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve known her since she&rsquo;s been little,&rdquo; Asher said. &ldquo;When your star pitcher looks you in the eye and asks you to come back, you don&rsquo;t want to let her down. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m really ready to retire, but it&rsquo;s amazing when you retire how many people it affects. My age is telling me to get out and my heart&rsquo;s wanting to stay with it. It&rsquo;s fun coaching these girls. We&rsquo;ve got a great group this year.&rdquo; Talawanda has already earned as many wins as it did last year in Guenther&rsquo;s first season as head coach. The Braves were 5-18 overall and 3-9 in the SWOC in 2016. Friday&rsquo;s performance aside, Guenther said the program is improving. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re not going to win the conference in one year, but we can have those aspirations and strive for it,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;We have six seniors, but we also have a lot of new kids on the team. One of our goals was to go .500 this year, and we want to win more games in the conference. &ldquo;This isn&rsquo;t a worst-case scenario. Our goals are not out of reach by any means, and I think our team has the ability to come back and make some noise. We&rsquo;ve been working a lot with the youth program. We&rsquo;re optimistic about the future.&rdquo; Both teams are scheduled for doubleheaders Saturday. Talawanda will be on the road to face Beavercreek, while the Cougars host Madison. Edgewood 210-604&mdash;13-14-2 Talawanda 000-001&mdash;1-4-8 WP &mdash; Sam Davis (8-3); LP &mdash; Kayli Scott (2-6). \n<!-- map content:  Everyone knows Jennifer Hudson has a powerhouse voice, but her son, David Otunga&nbsp;Jr., is a singer in his own right. McNicholas (8-7, 5-2 GCLC Central) took advantage of some Fenwick mistakes to gain the lead in the fourth, and that would decide the game. Rockets second baseman Michael Lang reached on an error to lead off and Brian Ford followed with a single. The twist? The scammers are using the names of actual deputies to solicit money from unsuspecting victims.&nbsp; &quot;It's sickening because a lot of times they're focusing on the elderly,&quot; said Chief Deputy Rob Streck.&nbsp; He has seen plenty of scams during his time at the Montgomery County Sheriff's Office. In the latest attempt, a caller claiming to be with the sheriff's office tells you that they either have a warrant for your arrest or that you owe money for skipping jury duty.&nbsp; They tell residents &quot;that they need to come downtown and put their credit card into a kiosk or go to Kroger and get a Kroger card and put money on, on this kind of card,&quot; he said.&nbsp; It's a scam that scares people into doing the wrong thing.&nbsp; Not only do the scammers say they work at the sheriff's office, they use the names of actual employees to sound more official to possible victims.&nbsp; &quot;The scam artists are smart enough to say, 'hey in this technology world somebody may be smart enough to Google somebody's name.' If they Google Rob Streck, one of the first things that pop up is a link to the sheriff's office website,&rdquo; the chief deputy said. Anyone who gets a scam call is urged to immediately hang up and call the sheriff's office to report it. businesses in the downtown area &ndash; was lifted Friday evening. Water sample results showed acceptable levels of chlorine residue. One of the 32 restaurants or business serving food that were directly notified of the boil alert by the city was TJ Chumps on East Linden Avenue, where business dropped off after the advisory was first issued, said Managing Partner Mike Leigh. &ldquo;It really causes some difficulty in the operations of the business,&rdquo; he said. FIRST REPORT: Miamisburg issues water boil advisory The level of chlorine in samples at the city&rsquo;s water treatment plant Thursday morning fell below Ohio EPA recommended levels because of a mechanical failure, prompting a boil advisory for about 1,900 Miamisburg water customers, many of them businesses, said city Public Works Director Valerie Griffin. The advisory area included about one-third of land in the city, Griffin said, and the mechanical failure is &ldquo;not associated with the construction&rdquo; of the city&rsquo;s $69 million water and sewer master plan. Those upgrades are being paid for by annual rate hikes that have caused customer criticism in recent months. At TJ Chumps, the boil advisory &ldquo;absolutely&rdquo; had some impact on the 10 to 15 percent drop Leigh estimated the restaurant witnessed since it was issued. With the city&rsquo;s &ldquo;antiquated&rdquo; infrastructure, Leigh said the water issues have occurred frequently enough for the restaurant to issue guidelines for operational changes, such as wash room procedures or switching from dispensed to bottled drinks. RELATED: Miamisburg customers question water bills On South Main Street, Mz. Pickles Sandwich Shop owner Carol Brassington said the boil advisory caused frustration, inconveniences and precautions. &ldquo;Even just to do the dishes&hellip;. you can&rsquo;t rinse the dishes in contaminated water so you still have to use bottled water to do dishes,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;It just makes things go so much slower and it&rsquo;s more difficult.&rdquo; More than 850 gallons of bottled water were distributed Thursday and Friday, Griffin said, as Sam&rsquo;s Club, Costco and the Ohio EPA assisted. The mechanical failure occurred overnight Wednesday or early Thursday morning and wasn&rsquo;t detected at the water treatment plant until about 7 a.m. Thursday. &ldquo;You can manually switch, but it&rsquo;s set up on automation and it didn&rsquo;t switch over,&rdquo; Griffin said. &ldquo;So our staff was able to do it manually&hellip;..unfortunately, some of that infrastructure is getting old and doesn&rsquo;t work as it&rsquo;s designed.&rdquo; &ldquo;As part of the water and sewer master plan, we will have a better alarm system and better communications put it at the plant. So hopefully it will create some redundancies and we won&rsquo;t have surprises like this,&rdquo; she said. The five-year water and sewer upgrades are designed to modernize Miamisburg&rsquo;s water and sewer system. The rate hikes they are being financed with have been a source of contention with many customers in recent months &ndash; as have the accuracy of the bills received from the city. Facebook posts by Miamisburg customers Thursday and Friday reflected city water issues remain a sore spot. &ldquo;This is unfortunately a byproduct of running a utility and providing water to your customers. Sometimes failures happen,&rdquo; Griffin said. &ldquo;Failures happen in any system, and we apologize for the inconvenience.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/miamisburg-businesses-feel-effects-water-advisory/5XBdyNYSrypvguW6Brg5HK/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyDaytonDailyNews/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18480743_042017msb-water-boil.jpg\" title=\"Miamisburg businesses feel effects of water advisory \" alt=\"Miamisburg businesses feel effects of water advisory \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/miamisburg-businesses-feel-effects-water-advisory/5XBdyNYSrypvguW6Brg5HK/\" target=\"\">Miamisburg businesses feel effects of water advisory </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t   \n\t\n\t\n\n\t\t Nick Blizzard , Natalie Jovonovich , <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>A Miamisburg boil water advisory affecting about 25 percent of the city customers — including many businesses in the downtown area – was lifted Friday evening.   \n<!-- map content:  Geneva Rhoden spent a year waking up, losing sleep and shedding more tears than she cares to count. &ldquo;I would like to say from a mother&rsquo;s heart, that I hurt so bad inside from the day that I found out,&rdquo; Rhoden said about the methodical executions of her sons Christopher Sr. and Kenneth, her grandchildren and grandson&rsquo;s fiancee. &ldquo;I think about it day and night.&rdquo; Rhoden and her daughter, Teresa Grebing, issued a gripping plea for answers Friday, a day before the anniversary of the Pike County murders of eight people. News of the killings a year ago today caught the eye of the nation for a moment. But as the cameras, satellite trucks and reporters left, what remained was a family in pain and a $10,000 reward for the tip to end their nightmare. The video &mdash; announced moments before its release by Attorney General Mike DeWine on WHIO Radio&rsquo;s Miami Valley Morning News &mdash; is a plea from the family for answers and more donations to the Crime Stoppers reward. For nearly 30 minutes, Rhoden and Grebing describe their loss in what DeWine called &ldquo;gut-wrenching&rdquo; detail. &ldquo;Please, please, please come forward,&rdquo; Grebing says with increasing emotion, crackling the audio. Between sobs, she expresses comfort in knowing her lost family members would make the same plea for them had fate cast a different lot. She professes hope in knowing some day &ldquo;the case will be cracked and we will have answers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Please end this nightmare we&rsquo;ve been living,&rdquo; she says. The Rhodens &mdash; Christopher Sr., 40, Christopher Jr., 16, Dana, 37, Gary, 38, Hanna, 19, Kenneth, 44, and 20-year-old Frankie &mdash; died April 22, 2016, with Frankie&rsquo;s fiancee, Hannah Gilley, 20, in four homes across Pike County. DeWine told WHIO&rsquo;s Larry Hansgen it is &ldquo;hard to tell&rdquo; if would-be tipsters are withholding information more due to fear of prosecution or retaliation for speaking out. But he doesn&rsquo;t &ldquo;think there&rsquo;s any doubt &hellip; there are people who know things they are simply not telling us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Anyone who has information and is afraid to give it to us because it might implicate them in some growing of marijuana or some sort of involvement in drugs, we want to assure them our focus is not on that,&rdquo; DeWine said. &ldquo;Our focus is on eight human beings who have been killed.&rdquo; But still, Pike County Sheriff Charles Reader said the fear of becoming another victim exists. &ldquo;The fear of retaliation from whoever these killers are, that&rsquo;s real,&rdquo; he said. Anyone with information on the murders is asked to call the Ohio Bureau of Criminal Investigation, 1-855-BCI-OHIO (224-6446) or the Pike County Sheriff&rsquo;s Office at 740-947-2111. To make a donation to the reward fund, write a check to Southern Ohio Crime Stoppers. In the memo line, indicate the donation is for the Rhoden murders. Checks can be mailed to Deputy Dave Weber, c/o: Ross County Sheriff&rsquo;s Office, 28 North Paint St., Chillicothe, Ohio 45601. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t about being one of the few Middletown products &mdash; perhaps only two &mdash; to have accomplished what he did last season. The best available research suggests that only Schwarber and Jerry Lucas have played on teams that won world championships in major sports. Lucas was a member of the 1972-1973 New York Knicks who won the NBA championship. Schwarber, of course, recovered from two torn ligaments in his left knee in time to hit .412 with a double, two runs batted in and three walks last fall for the Chicago Cubs team that ended its 108-year drought by winning the World Series in a seven-game thriller against the Cleveland Indians. The catcher-turned-outfielder remains too thrilled about the accomplishment to worry about where he fits into Middletown lore. He continues to feel simply lucky to have been able to take part. &ldquo;Being a competitor, that&rsquo;s what you dream about,&rdquo; Schwarber, 24, said Friday in the Great American Ball Park visitors&rsquo; clubhouse before the opener of Chicago&rsquo;s three-game series against the Reds. &ldquo;Your ultimate goal is to win the championship. For me to be on a team that, No. 1, got there and then to win it and have it happen at a young age &mdash; the organization and the players get all the credit just for letting me come on board and be able to contribute.&rdquo; Schwarber, a 2011 Middletown High School graduate, returned to Cincinnati as the Cubs&rsquo; regular left fielder and leadoff hitter. The left-hander was hitting .232 with three home runs and eight RBIs while having played in all 15 games for 8-7 Chicago. He had a .500 (7-for-14) on-base percentage leading off and drew a career-high three walks April 10 against Kansas City in his first regular-season game at Wrigley Field since Sept. 28, 2015. He was leading the Cubs with 11 walks. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s going good,&rdquo; said the 6-foot, 235-pounder. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t feel like I have to take a different approach. I take the same approach to at-bats and try to get on base and score as many runs as possible. That&rsquo;s a winning recipe.&rdquo; Manager Joe Maddon&rsquo;s reaction when asked his thoughts on Schwarber as the leadoff hitter? &ldquo;Love it,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s doing a good job. He&rsquo;s been hitting the ball hard and accepting his walks. He&rsquo;s doing a good job in the outfield. He&rsquo;s striking out a little bit, but we knew that. It&rsquo;s very scary every time he&rsquo;s swung the bat. He&rsquo;s done a great job.&rdquo; April 7 was the one-year anniversary of Schwarber&rsquo;s injury, and the only knee-related work he&rsquo;s doing these days is maintenance. &ldquo;Mainly to keep my hips loose and things like that,&rdquo; he said. He was as concerned about finding tickets for his family, nine of whom were due for Friday&rsquo;s game, and looking forward to spending Saturday night at home, getting to know his newest nephew, Roman, the son of his sister Kelly. He considers that a good way for him to stay grounded and not get caught up in the hoopla of the Cubs winning their first World Series since 1908. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m just trying to be the same kid from Middletown,&rdquo; he said, &ldquo;and keep grinding.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/sports/schwarber-occupies-rare-place-middletown-lore/L4rjuuUosWaB8n2ac4E1gO/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18485543_668918672.jpg\" title=\"Schwarber occupies rare place in Middletown lore \" alt=\"Schwarber occupies rare place in Middletown lore \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/sports/schwarber-occupies-rare-place-middletown-lore/L4rjuuUosWaB8n2ac4E1gO/\" target=\"\">Schwarber occupies rare place in Middletown lore </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nMark Schmetzer, <span class=\"story-source\">Contributing Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Kyle Schwarber isn’t too concerned about being one of the few Middletown products — perhaps only two — to have accomplished what he did last season. major employers &mdash; Miller Coors Brewing in Trenton and Wright Patterson Air Force Base in Dayton &mdash; gasoline prices in the Greater Cincinnati, Greater Dayton and Springfield areas will be about 12 cents lower per gallon than last summer. Officials from the Ohio Environmental Protection Agency persuaded the U.S. EPA that reductions the brewery and air base made in converting from coal to gas more than offset the benefits of using a cleaner-burning gasoline that, based on 2016 prices, costs about 12 cents per gallon more than the gasoline used across Ohio during colder months. MORE: Lifting of emissions rule could lead to local gas pump savings For about a decade, the U.S. EPA has required use of &ldquo;low Reid Vapor Pressure&rdquo; gasoline from June 1 through Sept. 15, Ohio EPA Director Craig Butler said. The change is expected to save motorists $44 million in Butler, Clark, Clermont, Greene, Hamilton, Miami, Montgomery and Warren counties. Federal officials approved use of the low-RVP gas as a substitute for the unpopular &ldquo;e-check&rdquo; program in which vehicles used to have their tailpipe emissions routinely monitored. &ldquo;Nobody liked that (e-check program),&rdquo; Butler told this news organization. &ldquo;We didn&rsquo;t like it either, and we found that as air-quality improved, we could switch to this low-RVP program.&rdquo; But low-RVP fuel turned out to be more costly than the expected. &ldquo;We were able to make a very scientific case back to the federal EPA that we can relieve consumers from this hidden expense, and still see all of the environmental improvements, by these two facilities changing their boilers to natural gas,&rdquo; Butler said. The U.S. EPA expedited Ohio&rsquo;s request, &ldquo;so we could give guidance to the refiners, that they would not have to make this gasoline this year,&rdquo; Butler said. The Sierra Club and the air force base did not respond to requests for comment. Denise Quinn, plant manager at the Trenton brewery, said, &ldquo;We are constantly evaluating our processes in an effort to operate more efficiently, as sustainability is a key priority at Miller Coors.&rdquo; The brewery in January of 2016 permanently shut down two coal-fired boilers that were installed in 1981 and were used to make steam for the brewing process. The company spent more than $10 million in switching to a natural-gas process partly to reduce greenhouse gas emissions, but also also to reduce costs &ldquo;and remain ahead of the curve when it comes to complying with increasingly stringent air quality requirements,&rdquo; Quinn said. Because of the change, the brewery&rsquo;s greenhouse gas emissions in 2016 decreased 37 percent; nitrogen oxide emissions droppedd 88 percent; and sulfur dioxide emissions decreased 96 percent, she said. Quinn added: &ldquo;The Trenton brewery is the first and only brewery in the country to receive recognition from U.S. EPA for achieving the Energy Star for Industry Challenge. To achieve recognition, the facility certified that it reduced the overall energy intensity of making beer by 10 percent.&rdquo; Meanwhile, at the brewery, Miller Coors has completed &ldquo;over 20 energy reduction efforts involving people and processes,&rdquo; and also has made numerous changes to reduce energy and water usage, Quinn said. The brewery in 2009 was the first Miller Coors brewery to become landfill-free, meaning, &ldquo;we reuse and recycle materials at a rate of over 99 percent&rdquo; Quinn added. &ldquo;The facility ships over 60,000 truckloads of beer per year, and for every five truckloads of beer produced, the brewery generates one truckload of co-products, recyclables and beneficially reused materials.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/butler-county-brewery-helps-lower-summer-gas-prices-for-motorists/KLj4oaBrbJJBVrHhVAOuKJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18473843_012817-wls-StraightTalk.jpg\" title=\"Butler County brewery helps lower summer gas prices for motorists \" alt=\"Butler County brewery helps lower summer gas prices for motorists \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/butler-county-brewery-helps-lower-summer-gas-prices-for-motorists/KLj4oaBrbJJBVrHhVAOuKJ/\" target=\"\">Butler County brewery helps lower summer gas prices for motorists </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nMike Rutledge, <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Thanks to pollution reductions at two major employers — Miller Coors Brewing in Trenton and Wright Patterson Air Force Base in Dayton — gasoline prices in the Greater Cincinnati, Greater Dayton and Springfield areas will be about 12 cents lower per gallon than last summer. Officials from the Ohio Environmental Protection Agency persuaded the U.S. EPA that reductions the brewery and air...</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t            \n\t\t        \n
